The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Neivspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin” will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ice
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we arc not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin O ffice Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, JUNE 27, 1891.
The Republic Comes Along.
It is safe to say that, up to the present, no
droller reading has been placed before the
New South Wales public than the leading
articles in the bulk of the Sydney journals
dealing with the probable result of the
general election of June 1891. What makes
these articles so attractive is their delightful
imbecility. When imbecility is kept in the
background or tied to a bed-post, as in the
case of gibbering George 111., or the late
mad King of Bavaria, the public passes
pityingly along and makes no sign. But
when, day after day, this hebetude obtrudes
itself in the leading columns of the metro-
politan papers, the case becomes serious and
demands attention. For the question
amounts to this, “is there any good reason
why the manhood of the oldest and, in many
Avays, the leading Australian State should
be diurnally treated to a rechauffe of politi-
cal fiction which, like the murder of Madame
Judy by her incorrigible husband, M. Punch,
is too transparently a ‘put-up job’ to
engender any feelings but those of merri-
ment in the spectators ? ” A little clowning
is good in its way. Like a mild cigarette it
helps digestion and tends to make men
agreeable. It is when the journalistic merry-
andrew claims to be serious and to demand
that his bald jokes should be received as
obvious truths that the public gets its back
up and becomes curious to know what
manner of men are these editors and writers
who appear to have such a contempt for the
understanding of the great majority of their
fellow-citizens.
* * *
The foregoing paragraph is suggested by
the fact that, up to the present moment, the
New South Wales capitalistic press wallows
in the poor affectation of believing that the
voice of the country as expressed at the
ballot-box favours Freetrade and the stability
of the Parkes Government. Now that voice
really means sudden death to the present
regime of sycophancy, and a short shrift
to free Foreign-trade as well as to that
bastard child of monopoly—freedom of con-
tract, as the phrase is understood by special
constables. It means much more. In simple
but precise words, which there is no misunder-
standing, this same voice proclaims that
government by trickery and chicane is in
need of extreme unction ; that Labour—
imperfectly organised as it is—has secured a
legislative recognition hitherto denied it,
and that the true Democracy of New South
Wales, if it but play its hand properly with-
out any attempt to revoke, has the future of
the State in its palm. Nor does the lesson
of the struggle stop here. For, in addition
to all this, the polling at the Sydney and
suburban booths alone writes upon the wall
some messages which require no Daniel to
interpret, which a child might understand,
but which the spooks of the Press decipher
iii a manner to make one pause and reflect
on the heights of impudence to which men
will scramble when tilled with the rancour
of commercial or class prejudice. Be it ours
to render into plain English a few of the
electoral messages so grotesquely construed
by the Mohawks of the daily Press.
# * *
To begin with, Parkes and his morally
ragged regiment must go—not with the
honours or war, with flags flaunting and
bands braying ; but with facings torn oft’,
uniforms tattered, and a couple of lusty pipers
and drummers bringing up the rear with a
melody appropriate to the occasion. This
must be the first move ; for progress is im-
possible so long as he and his clientele con-
tinue to hold down New South Wales as the
Lilliputians did the good-natured giant
Gulliver whom they caught asleep. That
he has held office so long and done so little
will for years be remembered as an apt
illustration of the small amount of know-
ledge combined with the large quantity of
charlatanism that is required to govern men.
His whole political life is but a dreary
record of successful chicane crossed and re-
crossed by meandering streamlets of slobber
oyer the advantages of monarchical institu-
tions and the virtues of a Royal Family
whose males travel the country as magsmen
while the females would scarcely stoop to
touch him or his with the handles of their
fans. True it is that Sir Henry Parkes
boasts of having done more than any of his
contemporaries to swell the bulk of the
colonial Statute-book. But an examination
of his measures shows that the good in them
is filched from unacknowledged sources,
while the bad is invariably his own. As it is
impossible to build a new house till the old
one is razed and the debris removed, so the
producers of New South Wales have at last
come to the conclusion that legislative ad-
vance or amelioration is an idle dream till
the knights and lacqueys who have so long
obstructed it are warned to up and clear the
way.
* # #
The second fact brought prominently for-
ward at the late ballot-box examination is
that the monarchical form of rule, with its
inevitable accompaniments of flunkeyism,
fawning servility and squalor is malodorous
to the nostrils of the great majority. In
this particular the Australian worker is
radically opposed to the Australian drone.
The latter, unable in most cases to reach
distinction by the legitimate means of
moral or intellectual worth, endeavours to
obtain it by trailing in the mire the poor
modicum of manhood bestowed on him by
nature. By a course of conduct mostly made
up of sycophancy he occasionally obtains
leave from an elderly lady in a distant land
to wear a cross on his breast and place three
or more letters after his name. Whether
the gods ever smile is a question that has
never been satisfactorily settled. But if
anything could force, not alone a smile, but
a guffaw from them it would surely be the
contemplation of a mature or elderly man
who took pride in being permitted to wear
a piece of crooked metal on his coat. It is
against such tomfoolery and its attendant
arrogance, as well as against the system
that encourages men to make such exhibi-
tions of themselves, that the proletariat
of New South Wales protest. They object
to be dragged down to the level of every
bourgeois that exposes Democracy to the
sneers of those who accuse its advocates of
greed for pinchbeck honours. We believe
we are but voicing the sentiments of the
Australian craftsman and labourer when we
assert that, however much they desire, as
we do, a welding of the provinces into one
homogeneous mass, the federation offered
them by the late Convention—a federation
which makes provision for trumpery titles
and a vice-regal court, presided over by a
sprig of royalty—is not the union they
desire. They see no reason for congratula-
tion in the prospect of a court of blowsy
knights and half-naked women. If Sir
Henry Parkes and hishangers-on wish to see
real nakedness in squalid juxtaposition with
the highest titles, let them go to the England
of which they mouth, and contemplate at
their leisure the three or four millions of
starving paupers, or the ten or twelve millions
who, from day to day, live within a month’s
march of the workhouse. Let them tell
these half-starved ones of Britain’s glorious
past pand of the high morals of the dynasty
which for 177 years has lived on the fat of
the land. Sir Henry and his henchmen can
then return and tell Australian workmen
what monarchy and courts and titles have
done to make the Briton contented and
happy.
* # #
The labour-vote of Wednesday last week is,
therefore, an essentially Republican one.
It is an unmistakable protest against intro-
ducing into a new country, of vast potentiali-
ties, the political and social absurdities that
are so rapidly decaying in the old land
where their roots seemed so firm in the soil.
For this gradual, but fundamental, change
in the ideas of the colonial artificer and
wage-earner we take to ourselves some
credit. For over a decade The Bulletin
has endeavoured, so far as in it lay, to show
that monarchy, aristocracy and sacerdotalism
were a trinity of wens on the face of
society, and that, so far from preseiviug
a community and making it prosperous
or contented, they began by destroying
its self-respect and ended by destroy-
ing itself We claim to have assiduously
watered the Democratic acorn which has
become such a goodly sapling and which
gives every promise of growing into a sturdy
oak. In its virility we never lost faith even
when at times some of its branches showed
symptoms of decay. Ihese symptoms have
passed away, and we hope to be pardoned
for a little exuberance on discovering by last
week’s vote how deep into the soil of our
adopted country the roots have struck. In
one sense a newspaper is like an individual.
When the latter finds the efforts of years
ultimately successful he forgets in that
success the anxieties and disappointments of
the struggle. So with a journal. When it
sees the tree it planted and tended
approaching a vigorous maturity the sight is
a welcome one. Without desiring to dwell
longer on this phase of the subject we can
honestly say that to no group of persons in
broad Australia was the phenomenal success
of last week’s labour-vote more pleasing
than to those connected with The Bulletin.
•* ' #
We have said that the labour-vote points
unmistakably towards a coming Great Aus-
tralian Federated Republic. This, after all,
is no matter for surprise. The sempiternal
drivel poured out by Sir Henry Parkes and
his satellites regarding the glories of England,
of her political and social system, her aris-
tocracy, her Parliament, and her monarch
had precisely the opposite effect to that in-
tended. In trying to precipitate Federation
in order to save himself from the “ creature
Protection ” which a couple of years ago he
affected to despise, he really gave Democracy
a lift on the road to fruition and adroitly
hoisted himself with his own petard. The
project of Imperialism which he had at heart,
of union “under the Crown”—the very
expression is fraught with future mischief—
of eternal Australian subordination to a
small, distant island, of squalid titles for
political apostacy, and of vice-regal rule
by a royal dummy, did much to awake
New South Wales workmen to the dangers
ahead. This awakening was materially
helped by the visit of Sir George Grey, on
whose record as a cultivated and consistent
democrat there is no stain. Grey, it is true,
has left Australia for his island home, but
not before he had planted ideas which ger-
minated with unprecedented rapidity. The
rapidity with which political thought has
ripened within a short time may be gauged
by recent labour-votes in New Zealand,
South Australia, Victoria, and New South
Wales. And if the Sydney ballot-boxes told
such a tale as they did in spite of hasty
labour organisation and of plural-voting, it
requires no prescience to reckon what their
story will be when organisation is complete
and bricks-and mortar disfranchised. Con-
sidering that in one of the smallest suburban
constituencies 500 plural votes were polled,
nearly all in favour of one candidate, the
outlook for readers of Riot Acts and their
abettors is by no means re-assuring. There-
fore, it is that the two things which Labour
must take in hand without a moment’s delay
are organisation and the single vote proposal.
Let the former be but complete while the
latter is the law of the land, and there lies
before the New South Wales wage-earner a
future such as his English or American
brother has never contemplated even to this
day.
• • •
Suppose that by some chance Sir Henry
Parkes remained at the head of affairs. Is
he sincere in his promise to take the initia-
tive and do his best to make One-man-one-
vote the law of the land 1 The question
answers itself. Was he ever in his life
politically sincere ? Has not his whole
career been merely a secular counterpart of
the ecclesiastical part played by the historical
Vicar of Bray ? The worthy Vicar, according
to the song, cared nothing for politics or
parties so long as he retained his living. The
worthy Premier may be safely accused or
congratulated on never having ikept a poli-
tical promise when there was'more to be
gained by breaking it. He began by fashion-
ing toys to his liking—he ends by endeavour-
ing to fashion men. Observe his tactics
regarding the abolition of plural-voting. In
his praiseworthy desire—praised by the
Sydney press—to make the measure a com-
prehensive one, he pleasantly yokes it with
the female-suffrage proposal. For the latter,
people are not ripe, or, better still, they are
so ripe that, if the community could be
represented by unity, those in favour of
woman-suffrage might be represented by
unity also, with the decimal point some dis-
tance to the left and five or six cyphers
intervening. The result is obvious. Out
goes the woman’s bill taking the man’s with
it as if to show that wherever there’s a
female there’s a male after her. So the
Premier sits complacently down, sends to
the papers the latest note he got from the
obscurest British nobleman, and declares he
would have passed the single-voting Bill but
for the Opposition. New South Wales
believes him, and, twenty years hence—he
puts down his prospective age at 120—-he
again rises in his Parliamentary seat to
protest that no man should have more than
one vote.
# * »
Perhaps of all post-election cries, the most
bizarre was Sydney Daily Telegraph’s
“ Freetrade and Federation are both safeas
if they were not mutually destructive. By
stipulating for one the other becomes im-
possible, and vice versa. The foreign-trader
who, in a congress of the six colonies, looks
for the triumph of his whimsical economic
fads, is not far removed in general under-
standing from the ingenious person who
tried to escap; from his shadow by running.
There remains the financial aspect of the
affair, or the effect of the situation on the
public credit. There is little doubt that the
conservative and plutocratic portion of the
Press will do its best, as it did in Victoria
during the Beery regime, to injure that
credit, by representing the new political
force as a disturbing element—a sort of
“Mountain,” such as appeared in French
politics a hundred years ago. But it will
not take the British creditor long to discern
that, the higher the plane reached by Labour,
the greater the stability of the State. Deter-
mined advances, it is true , will have to be made
on all sides. A bloody and insensate penal
code will have to be remodelled if only to
show that convictism and its ideas are things
of the past; from every page of the Statute
Book abuses call for reform or removal—
abuses that no PARKES-gang would ever
touch ; the whole rotten system of “ freedom
of contract ” will require to be subjected to
a searching analysis, and some movement
may be made towards controlling that liberty
of bequest which has done so much to per-
petuate superabundance on the one hand
and misery on the other. But all these
movements are essentially conservative in
the best sense of the word, and will end by
strengthening, rather than undermining, the
national credit. It should never be for-
gotten that years ago, in Victoria, the public
credit prospered in proportion as the Mel-
bourne Argus puled.
Compulsory Insurance.
This is conspicuously the age of “grandmotherly
legislation.” We do not recollect at the present
moment which fool it was who invented that
useful phrase, but the amount of service it has
done is incalculable. Grandmotherly legisla-
tion, in a general sense, includes all legislation
that is intended to promote the happiness and
well-being of the great mass of humanity.
Before this term was discovered the general
idea of law-makers was that the mass of
humanity had no necessity for happiness or
well-being, and therefore there was abundance
of law to authorise hanging, flogging, and trans-
portation, and to give a friendly lift to the
Church, and to shore up the landholder and the
monopolist, but at that point the duty of the
Legislature came to an end. Grandmotherly
legislation has included, among other things,
the factory laws, whereby the bloated and sin-
blasted Christian was no longer allowed to keep
children, Just emerging from infancy, at work
in a stifling atmosphere for sixteen hours per
diem ; it embraced the abolition of flogging in
the army and navy, the restriction of the grand
old hanging programme, the extinction of that
famous System which the old British Tory
established at Botany Bay, and many other
things of a like character. It is still raked up
as a term of reproach and contempt by the
Tory press, and is heaved at everything which
is done or attempted for the relief of human
misery, for the Tory press longs for a return to
the hyaena-like legislation of the old, blood-
stained past; and it is used to describe an
alleged tendency to national degeneration, which
is supposed to arise through the civilised world
having a porous-plaster supplied to it by the
Government, and having its feet put in hot
water, and a rag tied round its head at the
expense of the State. For it is a sort of un-
written axiom of the old, pig-headed school of
philosophy that the greatness of the British
people arose out of a certain coarse hardihood
which was made up indiscriminately of ignor-
ance, kicks, poverty, want of ventilation, hang-
ing, flogging, bull-fights, dog-fights, cock-fights,
drink, “ freedom of contract,” free competition,
the law of demand and supply, and all manner
of brutality and injustice in general. And,
therefore, when anything is attempted that
interferes with these established institutions, it
comes under the head of grandmotherly
legislation, by which term it is meant to be
understood that it is impossible in the first
place, and flabby and idiotic in the second.
• • •
The theory of compulsory and universal life
insurance, as a partial remedy for universal
poverty, comes naturally into this obnoxious
category. It is not Socialism in any strict or
well-defined sense of the term, but it has a cer-
tain Socialist tendency which arouses the un-
speakable disgust of the old, crusted Conserva-
tive. Therefore when the subject is mentioned
the Conservative organs usually treat it with a
certain pitying derision as a delusion that is too
glaring to require refutation, and placidly
deposit it in the dust-heap, along with astrology,
fetichism, Mumbo-Jumboism, or any other
busted myth of old-time ignorance. They deal
with it, in fact, as the average British journalist
deals with the subject of Protection—not by
advancing a single argument to ■ prove its
fallaciousness, but by airily taking it for granted
that everybody has long since recognised it as a
fallacy, and that its adherents—if they exist at
all—are merely a few unConsidered lunatics
whose opinions are sufficient evidence of their
derangement. Here and there, however, a
more than usually intelligent Tory descends
into figures and facts, and proves conclusively
that the idea of furnishing an endowment for
everybody’s widow and orphans, and supplying
an annuity for everybody who is too old to
work, is a hopeless impossibility ; but as the
same kind of Tory proved as conclusively in the
past that the locomotive and the steamboat
were delusions, and that the telegraph was a
lie, this amounts to very little. The cause of
this morbid tendency is not difficult to find.
The modern Conservative descends with a blast
of anger and ridicule upon every proposal for
abolishing or mitigating poverty, because he
lives upon poverty in all its shapes and varieties.
It is the poverty of the landless class which
strengthens the position of the landlord, for,
despite manhood suffrage and other reforms, the
party with the largest banking-account keeps
uppermost. It is the poverty of the worker
which is the salvation of the monopolist, for
nine strikes out of every ten collapse through
sheer hunger. And it is the grinding misery of


the countless thousands of old and partially
disused toilers both male and female,
which makes it absolutely certain that,
under present conditions, the supply of “free
labour ” will never run short. Therefore, the
monopolist howls with unearthly derision at
every proposition for the extinction of poverty,
and recommends a wide and Christian charity
as the only possible alleviation of human
suffering. And in doing this he is wise in his
day and generation. For as charity is a thing
that can be withdrawn at any moment, it
makes the receiver the abject slave of the
giver ; and, moreover, it serves as an advertise-
ment for the capitalist, and tones down the
public feeling against him, and also furnishes
him with a convenient brick to throw at his
detractors,' who, as a rule, are not charitable
through the want of the necessary assets. In
short, it is a virtue of which he has a close
monopoly, for it is the only virtue which a poor
man cannot exercise on any scale that is large
enough to get into the newspapers ; and, there-
fore, he booms it to the uttermost.
• * *
■ The shortest answer to the argument of that
stupendous liar, the Tory political economist,
is that the system of compulsory national
insurance has been tried, and with amazing
success. For over two centuries this policy
prevailed in and during that period
—so far as the artisan classes were concerned—
povei’ty ivets abolished. The insurance system
was established, not by the Government—for
no English Government ever possessed brains
enough for such an experiment—but by those
gigantic trades-unions, the mediaeval guilds.
No shearers’ union that was ever blasted by
the Argus, or cursed with the great anathema
of the S.M. Herald, wielded an authority
to be compared with that of these forgotten
institutions, and so long as they lasted, and
so far as their power extended, they created
a golden age for the artizans of England. There
was no “freedom of contract” in those days
for no one could practise any trade except by
joining the guild. There was no “free com-
petition,” for the blackleg was not invented,
and the guilds were strong enough to supply an
efficient guarantee that they would not be
invented. Also, there were no strikes, for the
guilds almost ruled the Kingdom in their palmy
days, and their internal strength made strikes
unnecessary, and therefore their funds were
devoted to maintaining the largest and most
comprehensive system of life-insurance on re-
cord. They provided for all their poorer
members in their old age. They maintained
the widows and children of all members who
had died leaving an insufficient provision
behind them, and they furnished education for
the young, and dowries for marriageable daugh-
ters. The system was compulsory in the widest
sense of the term, for the guilds levied sub-
scriptions upon all their members, and by a
process of “moral suasion,” which was very
much akin to excommunication, they included
among their members all the tradesmen of the
country. And, except among agricultural
labourers and a few other classes whose pur-
suits were still conducted on the grand old
principle of freedom of contract, they abso-
lutely abolished want and destitution, and
effectually gave the lie to the blatant assertion
that poverty is a necessary evil, to be alleviated
only by charity and pious drivel, and patience
and humility.
* • *
If the guilds had survived, the chances are
that they would have extended their operations
until at last they would have embraced all
classes of the community, and, if so, English
history would have been very different from
what it is. For then Freetrade and Free Com-
petition would never have been discovered, and
destitution would never have been invented,
and workhouses would have remained unbuilt,
and every human being in all the English
nation would have possessed an adequate pro-
vision for his old age, and have left his family
at least sufficient to preserve them from abso-
lute want. But Henry VIII. confiscated the
property of the guilds wholesale amid the tur-
moil of the Reformation, and he and his harem
and his fellow Reformers had the greatest burst
in all history upon the proceeds. That big
religious outbreak known as the spoliation of
the great monasteries was made by a wonder-
ful excess of zeal to cover the spoliation of
the great Trades-Unions also, and a dozen or
more of the noble families of England were
founded upon the savings which the artisan
class had accumulated in the slow course
of centuries ; the whole social system was
broken up, and in a few years the country
was flooded with thousands of homeless out-
casts, and then new laws were enacted against
poverty, and vagrants were hanged and flogged
and branded as the handiest remedy for the
evils which the Government had brought upon
them. And a little later the slave-trade was
invented by the infamous Hawkins, and then
the great soul of England rose to the occasion,
and the destitute inhabitants of the mother-
land were sold to toil in life-long servitude on
the Virginian plantations ; and as a fit appendix
to this great scheme the soul-blasting device of
State charity came into existence, and paved
the way for the huge monopolies which have
since made the English people mere beasts of
burden in the service of Capital. Trades-
Unionism has never recovered from that fatal
experience of three centuries ago. It is a mere
shadow of its former self, struggling feebly
against the “vested interests” which were
built upon the ruins and out of the plunder of
its ancient institutions.
• * *
Still the memory of that great mediaeval
experiment proves conclusively that poverty in
the main is curable, and that a system of uni-
versal and compulsory insurance is absolutely
practicable. And by reviving, on a far larger
scale, the methods of the old trades-unions a
great step can be gained. The policy of the
guilds provided for old men, widows, and
orphaned children, and if these three classes
were removed from the great industrial army
to-day the gain to Labour would be enormous.
The existing unions cannot absorb them or pro-
vide for them, and therefore cheap female and
child labour is superseding the male unionist in
a vast number of industries. This floating
quantity has hitherto defied organisation, and
it furnishes a supply of “ free labour ” which is
of incalculable value to Capital. A system of
universal State insurance, supported entirely
by taxation, would extinguish so much of that
hopeless poverty on which the accursed law of
Demand and Supply is based, that the indus-
trial problem would become comparatively easy
of solution. It would not in itself furnish the
solution, for nothing short of absolute Socialism
can ever remedy the evils that trouble humanity,
but as a means to the great end it would be in-
valuable. Anything that can alleviate penury
is a gain to the cause of Labour, for Monopoly
is built on the destitution of the masses, and
Capital lives mainly upon Want in its most
abject form. If it could be supposed, with any
show of reason, that such an alleviation would
induce the democracy to abandon all further
effort and rest content, then it would be better
that the experiment were left alone ; but if—as
we firmly believe—it would merely prove an
efficient weapon in the contest between incar-
nate Wealth and embodied Poverty, then the
advantage to be gained is worth almost any
price that could be paid for it.
» * *
The one apparently unanswerable argument
against the establishment of a system of State
insurance—a system so vast and comprehensive
that it would furnish a small but still a valu-
able provision for every citizen when incapaci-
tated by poverty and old age, and also support
thfe widows and children who arc left otherwise
destitute—is this : the cost of such a scheme
would bankrupt any nation which attempted
the experiment. Apparently this theory is un-
answerable ; in reality it is more empty than
air. Almost every nation in existence is even
now wasting its energy on experiments of a far
larger and more costly character, which pay no
dividends and yield no possible return, and
which are, nevertheless, regarded as matters of
comparatively small importance. The money
which England scattered in twenty short years
of frenzied warfare, for the purpose of extin-
guishing the liberties of France, would —if
properly applied have created a system
whereby destitution might have been abolished
in England to the end of time. If the
extinction of poverty throughout England be
impossible, then the Napoleonic wars were also
impossible, and if they went through the
empty form of taking place, that fact makes
not the slightest difference—they were impos-
sible none the less. The sum which France
fooled away in twelve mouths upon a useless
struggle with Germany would have done the
same good service to the French people, and thus
another impossibility is demolished. A moderate
tax upon land values would furnish ample
means for the great experiment in Australia—
in fact, the means are nowhere wanting, and in
any case the demand upon the nation would be
more apparent than real. For though the cost
of a scheme for the abolition of the destitute
classes may appear formidable, it must be taken
into account that these same classes already
live, in some shape or form, at the charge of the
State. The price of them may be reckoned up
in the cost of gaols, benevolent asylums,
hospitals, and similar expenses ; and as the
destruction of poverty would destroy, at the
same time, the whole degrading, soul-wrecking,
and ruinous system of charity the gain would,
in a great measure, counterbalance the loss.
The only reason why the project is impractic-
able is that this world of shams and military
idiocy seldom grows enthusiastic except over
projects of destruction. The “ spirits of our
fathers ” are ready to start from every wave
when the question at issue is the bombardment
of some helpless, forlorn, and unoffending
nigger ; they never start worth a cent in any
cause that is of the slightest service. The flag
that braves the battle and the breeze falls into
a cataleptic state except when it is floating over
some scene of slaughter, where the legal tender
of the Anglo-Saxon people is being blown away
in useless smoke. The great soul of the nation
rises nobly to the occasion when the occasion
consists of war and carnage and the blare of
trumpets and the thunder of the big but inane
drum ; it falls with a dull thud at all other
seasons. When the matter at issue is one that
concerns destruction and wholesale breakage
the word “ fail ” is nowhere to be found in the
bright lexicon of patriotic drivel; but at other
times the said lexicon is a limp and broken-
backed contrivance that is filled with impossi-
bility. And from these causes the scheme
which we have described is most probably im-
practicable. As a theory it is undoubtedly
feasible, but all the economic theories on earth
will not keep a nation which lacks the courage
to put them into practice.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Swindling the Nation.
A huge, ungodly swindle which, for its cast-
iron cheek and its tremendous assurance, has
very few equals, is being perpetrated just now
by the Victorian pastoralist at the cost of the
Victorian nation ; and the nation is so much
used to being swindled by the pastoralist that
it hardly even kicks at the process. Some
years ago, when that enormous stretch of
country known as the Mallee district was a
mere howling wilderness, immense tracts of
land were let out to squatters at a nominal
rental of 2s. 6d. per square mile, or about five
acres for a farthing per annum. This was long-
before the days of irrigation, and the value of
the Mallee country was then an unknown quan-
tity. But within the last few years it has been
discovered that this unconsidered waste is really
the richest land in the province, and when a
sudden demand for Mallee country set in the
local squatter longed to raise a boom in the
district, and to sell allotments, and dispose of
townships by auction. The one difficulty in
the way was the unpleasant fact that he had
not one single inch of land to sell, and was only
a lessee at five acres for a farthing, with a 12
years tenure, but this consideration was of
small account when a profitable speculation
was in view. All over the Mallee country the
joyous pastoralist put up the familiar legend —
“These allotments for sale by auction.” and
started gaily to sell off thousands of pound*
worth of land that he didn’t own, arid to trade
away the nation’s inheritance, and annex the
proceeds. The purchaser got the property at
about half its value in consideration of taking
his chance of being turned off afterwards, but
it was generally felt that if the Government
didn’t interfere with the Seller, who was the
primary swindler in the transaction, it could
not, in common decency, eject the purchaser,
who was only the secondary embezzler; and,
therefore, the allotments went off gaily, and
the land which was leased at 2s. 6d. a square
mile was sold at 15s. an acre, and the squatter-
man held high carnival on the proceeds. Then
the attention of the Lands Department was
“drawn” to this tremendous fraud, and that
office rose to the occasion. It didn’t arrest the
whole gang of speculators for doing the biggest
embezzlement on record and for obtaining enor-
mous sums of money on false pretences, and get
them ten years’ penal servitude apiece ; and-
failing this—it didn’t arrest the Government for
letting the nation’s property be stolen away
and thus becoming accessory to a fraud. The
Minister for Lands visited the district instead,
and, having viewed the proceedings on the spot,
he reported that—
It was merely a question whether the lessees should be
allowed to make money out of the land, or whether set-
tlement should be stayed until the land reverted to the
Government some dozen years hence.
This resolution is very like what a Minister of
Lands would be sure to say under such circum-
stances. Yet there are several considerations
in the case which are worth noting. (1) If the
Minister had leased his own laud instead of the
nation’s, and his tenant calmly collared the
property, and sold it as if it were his own, and
annexed the proceeds, the said Minister would
assuredly raise eleven separate kinds of Sheol
over the job, and tear after his tenant
with a warrant. (2) If there is any dire
necessity for trading off the last of the national
property right away, there is no need whatever
to wait till the present leases expire; the
lessee can simply have the short alternative of
giving up his lease and his plunder and quitting
the country, or else going to gaol for felony,
and the difficulty is settled in an instant. If
the land in question were the Minister’s own
property he would notice this circumstance—
unless he is every possible variety of a public
ignoramus. (3) Even if the country had to
wait a dozen years before selling the Mallee
lands it makes no possible difference. The
choice lies between getting most probably a few
million pounds twelve years hence, when the
increasing scarcity of land will have added
immensely to the value of this remnant of the
public estate ; or getting nothing either now or
hereafter, save the empty satisfaction of a boom
in the census-returns. And if it were the
Minister’s own land that was in question, it is
safe to assert that he would decide in such a
case to let the census-returns go to perdition or
any other convenient locality. (4) Even if the
country had to wait 120 years instead of 12 it
makes no difference either. For under existing
social conditions increased population brings
only poverty and misery, degradation, low
wages, the sweating-system, and similar evils,
and to give away the national estate gratis in
order to hurry up the national curse is the very
embodiment of insanity. And if the Lands
Department can realise none of these things it
would be interesting to know which politi-
cian’s uncle is in the Mallee swindle, for some-
body’s uncle is always involved when such
astonishing obtuseness is manifested.
The “Suicide Clause.”
The Ballantyne insurance case, in Melbourne,
has two morals, and they are both very large
ones. First, the insurance company which
retains in its policies the old conservative
proviso that the insurance shall be null and void
if the policy-holder dies by suicide, makes a
ruinous mistake; and second, the man who
insures under such conditions is an ass. The
deceased Ballantyne, of course, thought,
when he took out his policy, that the proviso
made no possible difference; the clause stated
that he must not suicide within two years from
the date of the insurance, and he had no idea of
suiciding at any time within ten centuries.
Unfortunately, however, he got drunk within
the forbidden period, and finished up by going-
out with a gun and depositing his brains in an
adjacent paddock, and then the company de-
clined to pay, and the usual expensive law-suit
followed. The “suicide clause,” of course,
was not actually meant to cover cases like
these ; it was drawn up on the supposition that
an insurance office was liable to be pervaded by
speculative individuals who habitually insured
their lives and then blew their heads off with
firearms in order to collect the money ; and as
an example of commercial lunacy it has few
equals. For it it is only once in several cen-
turies that the world produces a man who
suicides as a pure matter of business. The man
who actually does suicide, does it with a rush,
and has no time to ‘insure his life, there-
fore the class of individuals that the
suicide clause is aimed at is (like the
murderers who are deterred only by fear of
capital punishment) actually non-existent,
and the insurance company might as reasonably
insert a statement that it wouldn’t pay if the
insured had three legs. The man who insures
his life for the express purpose of blowing his
head off profitably as soon as he gets his policy,
may exist in fiction, but that is all—if there be
such an individual anywhere on earth the only
dead certainty about him is that he never will
suicide at all. He might prove a source of
income, in a small way, to "a liberal-minded
office, for now and then he might come along
and pay a tremendous premium to a company
which would allow him to die by his own hand
on the doorstep as soon as he got his receipt;
but the only probable result would be that the
company would be a premium ahead. But
though the stipulation that the assured must
not die by his own hand within two years is
absolutely worthless for the purpose it was
meant to cover, it is a man-trap which no one
should wilfully walk into. For nobody knows
when he may commit suicide, and the whole
bench of English bishops may go and lie down
in front of a train to-morrow under the influence
of some insane impulse that will never be
explained or accounted for. Suicide arising
through sudden insanity is as truly death
by disease as if the patient had come to
his end by slow consumption ; and the
man who undertakes that he will not destroy
himself virtually undertakes that he will not
be seized by a certain complaint; which is
about as sane a proceeding as if he guaranteed
that lie wouldn’t be killed through a houso
falling on him. Most of the leading Australian*
offices are now intelligent enough to recognise
this fact, and when the first premium is
accepted, the insured may swallow prussic acid
and die in the middle of the floor if he wantft
to, for all they commercially care. Most of th«
English offices, however, still plod on in the old
groove, and fill their policies with clauses
stating that the insurance will be void if the
policy-holder dies by suicide or by the hang-
man, or if he dies outside certain limits, or if
he perishes while drunk ; and as any man may
become irresponsible and commit suicide, or
may be hanged and his innocence proved after
he is dead, this sort of insurance is merely an
elaborate method by which a man spends his
whole life paying premiums, and ends by
leaving nothing to his family except a law-suit.
And a few of the more conservative American
offices still adhere to the “suicide clause,” and
thus furnish material for occasional disputes of
which the Ballantyne lawsuit'is an interesting
sample.
A few days ago three individuals were charged
before the Bendigo (Vic.) Court of Petty Ses-
sions with having neglected to destroy the
rabbits on their selections, and the Law, which
never loses a chance to demonstrate its superla-
tive idiocy, acted up to its usual character.
The solicitor for the defence set up the plea
that there was no such thing as a Bendigo Court
of Petty Sessions. There had been such a Court
at Sandhurst, but Sandhurst was abolished, and
therefore there couldn’t be a Court there any
longer’, and nobody had inserted an advertise-
ment in the Government Gazette stating that
there was a Court at Bendigo. Therefore,
though the Court was there, yet it wasn’t
there; and Sandhurst was officially gone, and
Bendigo hadn’t officially arrived, and conse-
quently there was no place at all thereabouts,
and the case must fall through in consequence.
And the case did fall through there and then.
The town was the same as before, and it was
the same Court, and the officials hadn’t changed
in a iiy way, but for want of an advertisement
stating that these things were there it was
resolved that they were non-existent. But
though the Law thus proved itself to be an ass,
it was only part of the animal—it was too great
an ass to know how to be a whole or con-
sistent mule. For the whole disturbance was
based on the assumption that the three defend-
ants were before the Court, but as there
wasn’t any Court therefore they couldn’t be
before it, consequently they must have been
somewhere else, and as they weren’t anywhere
else it naturally follows that they were
nowhere, which was a sufficient proof that
there were no defendants at all. Also, The
Court went beyond its pow ers in dismissing the
charge, for if there wasn’t any Court then there
w r as nobody there to dismiss anything; and
there wasn't anything to dismiss, for a charge
can’t be before a Court that isn’t there ; and a
Court that isn’t there can t discharge a case,
that is absent. Moreover, the solicitor’s case
broke down according to his own showdng, for
he laid his argument before the Court and at
the same time proved that there wasn’t any
Court, and if there wasn’t any Court then his
argument couldn’t be before it, and there being
no argument before it nothing had taken place
at all. Three-fourths of what is miscalled
justice now consists of technicalities, and most
of them are about as foolish as this Bendigo
stupidity.

Here is an instructive bit from the Canadian
Dominion. The Manitoba Government intro-
duced last session, into the local legislature, a
measure taxing all corporations operating in
provincial lands, railways, etc., and carried
it. The Federal Government vetoed the act,
and in April the Manitoba Government re-
introduced it and re-carried it, with a certainty
of its being re-vetoed. Conflict is inevitable—
with the final result certain to be in favour of
the Federal Government, because it is backed
up by the Imperial Parliament, yet there is no
question that equity is on the right of the
Manitobans, inasmuch as they are resisting the
exploitation of their territory by British capi-
talists. The provincialists are really fighting
against the creation of an absentee landlordism,
and, as such, they are contending for a right
which goes down to the root of their prosperity.
Yet, under the Constitution, they will have to
go to the wall. Now, a similar state of things
might arise here, and under the Convention
Bill, could only be settled by the Federal
Government, which, under the Bill, will simply
be an agent for Imperialistic syndicates, in a
way antagonistic to the desires of a State’s
people. Thus, we have another argument for a
careful survey of the Federal path before we
begin to tread it.

Supreme Court Judge Innes (N.S.W.) is now
sneering at “ servants,” forgetting that he him-
self is a servant of the State and a rather poor
one, too, considering that he gets £3OOO a year
from the State Treasury. We refer to his
Honor’s remarks in a recent master and servant
case wherein he apologised for making use of
the terms r “ master and servant,” which, in
these days, Seemed, he said, “to be objection-
able to some persons. In fact, judging from the
signs of the times, in a short while the latter
class would be styled ‘partners’ or ‘helps,’
and would have to be placed on a footing of
perfect equality with those who had to keep
them both.” Now, a turn for sarcasm in a
Judge is a very dangerous thing—for the Judge,
especially when exercised in a democratic com-
munity, and we respectfully but firmly advise
Judge Innes to stick severely to his judicial
functions, and leave social homilies severely
alone. That he and all others concerned should
accept this well-meant caution in the spirit in
which it is offered is all the more expedient by
reason of the fact that the time is not far dis-
tant when all Australian legal officials’will le
selected by a process in which the wishes and
opinions of the electors will be a much strong, r
factor than under the present system.

“Evangeline,” now kicking up its heels
at Sydney Criterion, is usually styled an opera,
but is as much akin to it as “ Widow O’Brien,
Esq.” is to comedy. Gorgeously staged, and full
of lively, if tinkly, music, its libretto is sicker
than that of most pantomimes, and the enter-
prising MacMahons might have had it fitted
with better “ talk ” through the local expenditure
of ten pounds. Here and there the business
descends to the level of white-washed nigger-
farce, and the ridiculous, mi-lmmorous heifer
should be re’egated to the theatrical dust-
bin. Fanny Liddiard undoubtedly annexes the
Abernethy for vocalism, while Johnny Gour-
lay scores heavily as a laugh-provoker, but Harry
Leston as Dietrich —Snazelle’s old part— may, in
time, push him hard. The Lone Fisherman of
Joseph Harris is a clever piece of pantomimicry
that hit the audience hard on first night, especially
the weird dance with its frantic finale, modelled
on the old Rocky-Roads-to-Dnblin, will-I-ever-
get-home-1-dunno mode of progression, an
apparent travestie on the “ County Fair ” horse-
race. George Fortescue’s Catherine seemed to
please the house vastly : tastes differ, of course,
in all things, and if the public are satisfied when
a humble critic is not, it only shows that the
latter's mental standard wants readjustment.
Virginia Earl is better placed here than elsewhere,
and her song. “I like it,” was a big go chiefly on
account of her distinct enunciation. Salvini has
a good voice and D, as a rule, funny. None of
the other folks have any great opportunities of
distinguishing themselves, but the shapely
limbs and sweet smile of one young American
lady have already ravaged the front stall-
rows where the connoisseurs in feminine flesh
do chiefly congregate. The Amazonian ballet,
shorn of its due proportions by scant stage-room,
is one of the best things of the kind yet seen here.
One quartette alone is worth a pilgrimage, and the
whistling chorus of “The wind pipes free” is
ingenious and attractive. By the way, the fisher-
man is the only one of his tribe we’ve seen who
doesn't smoke, swear and swizzle. On first night
Conductor Rice attempted to buttonhole the
audience by handing up bouquets, but they goft
tired of it, and so did he.
* *- *
The Sydney theatre-going public are
greatly indebted to Janet Achurch for interpret-
ing Sara Bernhardt, in advance, at Sydney
Garrick, by playing English versions of some of
her favourite characters. After reviving “Forget-
Me-Not” for two nights—with Herbert Flem-
ming in place of Charrington, as Horace Welby,
and Harry Power, as Malleotti, in place of George
Leitch—“Adrienne Lecouvreur,” Herman’s adap-
tation of Scribe and Legouve’s play, was staged on
Saturday for three nights, and will bo superseded
by “ A Doll's House,” with which the Chaning-
t m season will conclude on Friday evening.
Manager Cunard has already gone ahead to make
dates at Goulburn, Wagga, Albury, Geelong,
Adelaide, and Broken Hill. On their way to
England the company will probably play in West
Australia and at Colombo. Adrienne is one of
Janet Aclmreh’s best impersonations. For some
unknown reason she seems suddenly to have taken
to heart The Bulletin's oft-repeated counsels, and
has eschewed her usual exaggerations in the way
of glaring, gurgling and stage-tramping, and the
result is a fine and harmonious impersonation of
the generous, misunderstood and illusedpctof the
Comedie Francaise. The Maurice de Saxe of
Herbert Flemming is brightly and evenly played ;
his affectation of mirth when the husband banters
him aboutintriguing with his mistress instead of his
wife, having the ringof spontaneity. As Miehonnct,
a sort of Triplet, Chas. Charrington’s chin and
voice were, as usual, very much in his way, never-
theless, lie got two well-earn'ed rounds of applause
for the pathos of his green-room scene. There is a
quaint flavour about every tiling done by Gerald
Moore that draws a laugh even for the most
commonplace lines, and so his Abbe de Chazeuil,
for no especially evident reason, was very popular
with the house. Emma Bronton tried hard to be
vindictive and wicked as the Princess de Buillou,
a lady whose husband is always in the consomme,
but she’s not built that way—virtue in distress is
her forte. Alice May, Meta Pelham, Wm. Hol-
man, and Harry Power filled small gaps credit-
ably. “Our Flat” goes up on Saturday night
supported by Laurence Cautley, Hans Phillips,
Charles Brown, H. Bennett, Marie Brooke, Mrs.
Hill, Miss Gallagher, E. W. Royce, Edwin Kelly,
W. F. Clitheroe, Mary Kingsley, Polly Emery,
Gertie White.

Despite rain and elections, business has
been so unvaryingly good with the Opera Com-
pany at Sydney Royal that “ Gondoliers ” could
be run at least another fortnight profitably. So
it has been decided not to break up the season,
but to run it for all it is worth till its conclusion
on 7th July.

completely unroofed the main balcony at Congee
Aquarium, and did much damage to the main
building.

* * * St
The third French week at Melbourne
Princess’ was the same as the preceding two in the
matter of attendance, if not more so. For
the first (Monday’s) performance of “ Adrienne
Lecouvreur ” there were plenty of seats to spare
downstairs and in the amphitheatre, but this
apparent re-action collapsed as soon as it had set
in, and subsequent nights found people paying
their money for the proud right of standing up.
“ Frou-Frou,” with nothing in the way of scenery
to it, was so played as to move most of the house
to tears. The scene between the two sisters—in
which they pour out their feelings at a pace which
defies Pitman’s system, and would kill a type-
writer on the spot—will be talked of in Melbourne
when the maidens of to-day are mixing powders
for their children’s children. Bernhardt’s per-
formance of Gilberte, the spoilt wife, represents
the character in a light softer and more redeeming
■ than she has been shown upon the English stage.
Shelias, too, the artistic advantages of a delight-
ful sister in Jane Mea, and a grand, giddy papa,
in Munie. The latter comedian, here and in
“ Adrienne,” proves himself able to put feeling as
well as humour into his work. One of the best
of the quiet effects is made, by the typical old
Parisian buck, when, pulled up short by the sight
of Gilberte’s distress, he tells her that a parent
like himself seems no worse than “careless” so
long as she is contented, but “abominable” if she
be unhappy. “Frou-Frou ” contains more good,
penetrating morals than six ordinary plays; yet,
Brutus—or, rather, Varley—says it is indecent,
and Varley is an honourable man. The only fault
with “Adrienne Lecouvreur ” was a wooden lover
for Sara, who seemed to have cast one of the
weakest members of the company for the part of
Maurice de Saxe. Her devotion to this uninterest-
ing person is beautifully depicted, however, and
the tragic story of the poisoned actro s made a
profound impression in at least two of its scenes.
“ Jeanne d’ Arc ” (which we didn’t see) appears
to have been another triumph for the artiste, as
well as a spectacular success, whilst “Camille”
held them enthralled, as usual, on Saturday
night. A bare mention of a Bernhardt
performance can be accepted as equivalent
to a column of praise. This gifted woman has
educated Melbourne public opinion to a complete
understanding of what naturalness on the stage
really means, and it will be many a long day
before another Mrs. Brown-Potter can attract by
means of overdrawn caricatures of the great
original. Last week’s plays, not being of the
Sarclou series, were each published in acting form
—line for line in French and English. These
books are cheap at a shilling, and people who
read up a play beforehand, first in English and
then in French, need very little knowledge of the
language to enable them both to appreciate every
development of the story and pick up a few ideas
of Sara’s native tongue in its native turns. By
the way, the divinity displayed some expensive
millinery in “Adrienne,” and her face “left
nothing to be desired ” (as the dailies would say)
in any of the young characters. But the waits
were pretty bad again. Sara doesn’t mind the
play starting at 7.30, so long as she can enjoy her
tram-rides between the acts.
* * *
Produced on Monday night under most
irritating conditions, “Theodora” must be pro-
nounced a complete artistic success. The story
possesses stronger dramatic interest than “Cleo-
patra,” and gives Bernhardt a wider range of
opportunities to display her consummate ability.
Sardou has, in fact, supplied a phenomenal
actress with a phenomenal character, and one
which, whilst seeming impossible, is not altogether
inconsistent with historical tradition. Bernhardt,
who wears some beautiful dresses, is imposingly
supported by Duquesne, Darmont, Angela and
others, and the mounting of the show does credit
to the management. The sixth scene, Justinian’s
box in the Hippodrome, introduces a gorgeous
spectacle, and the subsequent rising of the popu-
lace, culminating in the arrest of Theodora’s lover,
is realistic to a degi’ee, but many people had
ere then gone home, disgusted by much waiting.
Indeed, the actual finish of the tragedy at ten
minutes past twelve was witnessed by little more
than half of the original audience ; yet even then
Bernhardt had her faithful admirers under sub-
jection, and when she offered her white throat to
the official strangler she sent one or two females
into hysterics. “ Theodora,” strung out into
seven acts, contained two hours of fascinatingenter-
tainment, scattered amidst two and a half hours
of intervals on its first representation. For this
insufferable state of things Madame Bernhardt is
alleged to be solely responsible ; the intervals can
be shortened if she chooses, but she does not
choose and she never will choose, until the audi-
ence rise in a lump and show their backs to a
genius who practically spits behind the curtain at
the toleration granted to her unamiable caprices.

although “ Moths,” poorly mounted and cramped
up on the smaller stage, was far less effective than

Idler,” on Saturday, drew a crowded house, and

cluded. On Monday, the irrepressible “ Betsy ”
began to dominate the situation with a feather-
brush, and levy blackmail on a disordered house-
hold until the burden of her existence became too
much for the other people to bear. “ Betsy ” was
always a lively girl in Pattie Brown's hands, and
with Brough, Titheradge and Anson playing old
parts, the show is one long scream as usual.

That season of comic opera so recently
“ inaugurated ” at Melbourne Opera House is to
get no further than “ Poor Jonathan,’’apparently.
Proceedings began with Jonathan, and as the
public resolutely refused to rise at him, a
number of other comic operas have been with-
drawn before they were produced. It is to be
feared that Miss Clara Mori vale’s season has not
yielded a bounteous harvest. Next Saturday will
introduce Jennie Lee, just back from New
Zealand, in the three-act comedy “ Run Wild.”

“ The Trapper,” as produced at Melbourne
Alexandra, on Saturday, turned out to be a less
coherent kind of Wild West Show than “Tile
Scout,” and will require any number of alterations
and repairs to make it stand for the anticipated
six weeks. Little or no use is made of the tank,
nor are the Indians presented under rational eon-
ditions. Beer and other refinements of civilisation
seem to be knocking all the sense out of Carver’s
redskins, who simply form themselves into a
grinning mob, and wait for the cowboys and
Mexicans to come and stoush ’em. Their war-
paint "and feathers are wasted in this instance,
seeing that they don’t perpetrate a weird dance in
the moonlight, or do anything worthy of an ancient
race. They might just as well be lying drunk at
the bottom of the tank. The plot of “ The
Trapper ” consists of a few decayed bones that were
buried instead of Lily Dampier a longtime before
the curtain drew up. A tiring old man, with a
. terribly big round head on him, tells a long yarn
about these remains, one syllable at a time, and
an elderly female of shattered intellect is always
attempting to confess something concerning them,
whereupon a husband with a lot of stuffing up his
back generally grabs hold of the unfortunate lad}’,
so that things get no forrarder. But, fortunately,
the plot makes no difference, and in point of fact
there isn’t one at all. In the second act a bar-
room scene is enlivened by the entry of Carver
and Lily Dampier on horseback, the damsel riding
her pony astride, after the manner of her sex in
Wild West regions. Carver is about to marry
Lily, it transpires, and in a tender, sentimental
mood they sit down to play euchre, after which
they shoot the necks off the bottles in the bar and
put bullets through the electric-light until
the Sheriff arrives with the Riot Act. Then
Carver’s dear old white horse lobs in, just like the
Christian it is, and gently butts him home to
his virtuous couch with its affectionate old head.
This it a fine touch of animal nature entirely.
Nothing else in “The Trapper” goes for much
except a tableau at the. end of the third act.
“ After the Battle ” shows Dr. Carver and Lily
leaning on their rifles to contemplate a really
artistic arrangement of dead Indians, cowboys,
Mexicans and horses, that lie strewn on the gory
field. The heaving of the horses’ sides is the only
visible sign of life in the picture, and those well-
trained animals are as defunct as Julius Caesar in
all other respects. Mr. and Mrs. Dampier have
parts in “The Trapper,” but the show doesn't
contain any acting opportunities worth men-
tioning. ,

The Gaiety Company, who have been wait-
ing around and cooling their heels for three
weeks, will make a start at Melbourne Princess’
on Saturday next (27th inst.)., The burlesque
appointed for that occasion is “ Ruy Bias and the
Blase Roue,” but the name of a burlesque doesn’t
matter much so long as the legs are there. Every-
thing must have a name, however, and this is
given as a guarantee of good faith. Of the
principals that were favourites last trip, five will
reappear on Saturday—Fred. Leslie, Nellie Barren,
Danby, Fred Storey and Sylvia Grey.
At Poverty Point.
Varley writes to the Aon about Bernhardt. He doesn’t
object so much to “ Camille” as to “Tosca” and “Frou-
Frou ” horrors.
Ladies in the Melbourne stalls, -seeing Bernhardt, do
■actually take their hats off. Excellent fashion, begun at
London Savoy.
Just as Bernhardt’s scenery is fetched from Paris, so
did MacMahon make one of his first rises by fetching to
Melbourne Irving’s scenery of “Romeo and Juliet,”
enshrining Miss Reid, the actress, with Cates as Romeo,
which Beilew had been acting all over Britain, in front of
Irving’s scenery.
Sara Bernhardt chops all round the troupe for leading
man. Fleury, Duquesne, Darmont, and Rebel have re-
spectively had a show as Armand Duval, Scarpia, Antony,
and Maurice de Saxe, in “ Adrienne Lecouvreur." This
provokes emulation ; and Angelo, still in the Co., was
her leading man in London ten years ago. Darmont has
the pull in a colossal kind of size, but womankind don’t
And any of the premiers good-looking, save Fleury, and
he only in modern costume.
Sara’s Melbourne repertoire is shorn after all, but people
appear to agree there will be a return visit, despite your
positively last and most positively last. So far it has
been ‘Camille,’ ‘Tosca,’ ‘Fedora,’ ‘Cleopatre,’ ‘Adrienne
Lecouvreur,’ ‘ Frou Frou,’ ‘Jeanne d’Arc,’ with ‘Theo-
dora,’ enough to carry her right through. Well, it is
really useless her acting anything so dry as the classic
‘ Phedre,’ though she may be perfect in it. Four of the
tragedies she played in Melbourne are by Sardou—
‘Camille’ is by Dumas; ‘Adrienne Lecouvreur,’ by
Scribe and Legouve; ‘ Frou Frou,’ by Meiihac and
Halevy ; and ‘Jeanne d’Arc,’ by Barbier.
Bernhardt is most generous taking her calls. Always
brings an actor on, even such as Munie, after his oire
scene as the Messenger, in “ Cleopatre.” Indeed, Munie
does that bit capitally, his irrepressible touches of terrified
humour being a welcome leaven.
The good stage memory will forget a part, but the bad
memory clings on to the greatest rubbish, like a limpet to
the rock, when once acquired.
French actors don’t wait forcues. They cut in, interrupt
the speech before its end, in the same natural way as you
do when talking. The dialogue in the Bernhardt plays is
a regular gabble, though the talking is beautifully
musical, almost operatic in its rhythm.
New York Times sketches J. A. Bailey, Barnum’s part-
ner. Was in Australia, with Cooper and Bailey’s show,
and worked up from an orphan farm-boy. The show made
money in Australia, but lost it in South America. Bailey
made a rise by buying Dockrill’s Circus, mortgaged to
Reilley, the ’Frisco show-printer, and Bailey got it for a
comparative song, whereupon he worked up such a rival
to Barnum as would have crushed that Bismarck had he
not overtured for partnership. For ten years Bailey has
managed the concern, Barnum being only a figurehead,
and Bailey bought Jumbo even without Barnum’s know-
ledge.
An old-time Victorian solicitor, now resident in Sydney,
pleasantly tells of a little experience he once had in the
dress-circle of the old Melbourne Royal. With his wife,
he occupied seats in the circle. Having to go out between
the acts, possibly to “see a man,” he naturally expected
to find his seat vacant when he returned, but he did not.
It was occupied by an individual who refused to leave.
The solicitor did not in this case consider possession nine
poiids of the law, but bundled the intruder neck-and-crop
out of the seat and down the stairs, in presence of the
ushers, who stood by without interfering. Result- an
action at law, in which the attorney got worsted, the
damages (£500) and costs amounting to upwards of £lOOO.
The seats were dear at the price.
, Old Jim Byers relates an amusing anecdote of his early
Australian sock.-and-buskin days. In the good times on
OulgougJim was doing “ Richelieu.” The luggage had
been delayed and Jim felt fixed. The hotel at which he
put up had a neat parlour, every chair in which was
adorned with an anti-maecassar. These Jim quietly
secured. The next visit the lady made to her parlour
she found all her treasures gone and immediately gave
information to the police. What was the astonishment of
landlady, police, and “pros.” to find Bulwer Lytton’s
clerical hero dressed from head to foot in anti-maccassars!
He had tacked them together and they passed off for the
lace robe usually worn by the representation of the
Cardinal. On another occasion he secured all the white
table-cloths as a toga for the noble Roman “ Virginius.”
Tcarle, the tragedian, acting Macbeth in England
lately, slopped almost in tears, because a man in the
audience was reading a newspaper.

The French Troupe, Sara’s, are all agog over the
Sydney prospect. Melbourne is the earthly paradise, but
Sydney is the heavenly, and they did so like what little
they saw of it. Even Daniel does not choke them off.
Lohr has been playing it very low down on the Maori"
landers. While ahead of Madame Patey he came across
a photograph of three infants—triplets—ranged in a row.
The happy father had bad them photographed with an
idea of claiming the “Queen’s bounty.” Since Lohr was
in Maoriland last ho has become a happy father, and
naturally there are many enquiries as to the youngster
and its ma. Lohr has had the Maori triplets transferred
to a Sydney' photographer’s card, and whenever any
Maoriland pressman inquires after the baby, he pulls the
card out from a pocket case, and with the beaming smile
of a happy father, says ; “Baby ! why', man, there’s three
of ’em!” The “genial one” is looked upon with awe
and respect in consequence. As the Maoris say, he is a
“ tangata kaha.”
Marian Burton, who has just come over to join the
Hallos, has been enjoying the drum cum dig. at Nathan’s
big house opposite Government House, Auckland. Water-
low is quite well, and so is baby, which is “so like paw.”
Williamson and Garner were never so astonished in
their lives as by the rush for seats for the Gaiety first
night. Nellie Barren and Leslie were not new - they had
been here lately, yet the furore surpassed that over Bern
hardt, and warranted Irving and Terry coming by next
boat. All this shows Melbourne to he unquenchable in
thirst for first-class stuff. Fancy- the Bald Brigade bat-
tering at the shop-shutters at seven a.m. !
Two features of the Bernhardt season are occupying the
minds of Melbourne theatre-goers, says a Melbourne cor-
respondent-one, the dreadful character of the imported
scenery which has marked her season, and the other the
marked absence of patronage from the vice-royalties who
are landed by the Austral Salon and endured by the hoi
polloi,
Joe Reynolds was 40 years at the London Britannia
theatre. When he was dying the clergyman asked if he
had any request, “Yes, please. Put on the tombstone,
‘buried under the management of Mrs. S. Lane.’”
Cruel. Thirteen years ago, as we have already noted)
Lord Desart divorced his wife, because of adultery with
Sugden, the actor, who married her, but now she has been
compelled to obtain a divorce from him. It is the story
of “ Frou Frou,” in real life, twice as painful, for the
actor acted with cruel heartlessness. Yet perhaps Desart
had trouble to make her go and see Sugden in the first
instance.

Signor Ferrari, to Melbourne Public Library.
Big Eliza, of New York’s World Museum, has a bust of
118 inches. It takes 40 yards of silk to make her a dress-
She is 37 inches above the elbow, and when she becomes a
bride, her husband will only be able to clasp one-third of
her at a time.

Tom Thorne, London manager, went with a friend to a
fair and won a lot of penny cigars at a cockshy. He gave
them to an old man at an adjoining show-, and the old
man’s little daughter said, “They will do for father.”
Next day Tom and his friend went round, but the girl
was alone. “ Aha!” says Tom, “ they’ve done for father.”

Barry Sullivan’s manner of speaking was very stilted,
but he does not seem to have been aware of the fact, as
he is said to have addressed to an actor the advice :
“ Speak naturally, sir, as Ido.” Once, when Pinero was
rehearsing a part not to Mr. Sullivan’s satisfaction, he
exclaimed: “Can’t you play it as I tell you, sir?” “ No,”
was the reply. “ I get 30s. a week ; if I could play it as
you tell me, I should want the same salary that you
have.”

banquet that harmonious snob and toady. Sir Arthur
Sullivan, felicitously said (the adverb is Sydney Echo’s)
that “ Among our own people no one who has visited the
Greater Britain beyond the seas but must be alive to the
depth of feeling stirred by the first bar of ‘ God Save the
Queen.’ It is not too much to say that this air has done
more than any other single agency to consolidate the
national sentiment which forms the basis of our world-
wide empire.” Sir Arthur Solomon (who must have been
stuffed by his friend, Composer Cohen) is a prevaricator.
When Australia can’t get away from “God Save” it
groans ; when it can it simply jams its hat on and “ gets ”
quickly.

Says Hawk: —“The Gilbert-Oellier combination has been
irretrievably broken up. It happened in this way.
Cellier, when travelling with B. C, Stephenson in Aus-
tralia, received his libretto from Gilbert, and on perusing
it discovered that the locale was similar to that of one he
was doing with Stephenson. He wrote this fact to
Gilbert. The answer was a telegram, short, though
perhaps, none too sweet: ‘Return my libretto at once.
Gilbert.’ ”

The title, “The Infant Mozart”—it always suggests
“ The Boy Politician ” —was suggested by James Smith,
of the Arhus. J.S. then held a transmigration-of-soul
sort of belief (one of his notions was that his own son had
received the soul of the Redeemer), and it was revealed
to him that the soul of Mozart inhabited the body of the
child Hutcheson. The subsequent conduct of his own
progeny (a very good fellow, by the way) suggested to
him that there must be a flaw somewhere in his reason-
ing, and he holds the belief no longer.
Ernest Hutcheson once found a “refuge and a home”
at R. S. Smythe’s, who thought of educating the “ young
Mozart.” One morning, however, upon getting up for
his morning stroll, about 1 p.m., the “Much-Travelled”
discovered that his front newly-painted garden-wall was
stencilled from one end to the other, in letters 9in. long,
“The Infant Mozart Lives Here.” This, the work of an
irate father, was too much for even R.S.S., who instaoter
transferred his charge to tub-thumper Torrance.
Alluding to the independent attitude assumed
by a growing Democracy, Francis Abigail,
it is said, once remarked in his Ministerial
days ; “ It's all along o’ this ’ere manhood
suffrage, tho’, of course, one durs’n’t say
so on the ’listings.” The latest stride made
by Labour is, however, not only a conse-
quence of manhood suffrage, but of payment
of members a measure whose existence
is now deeply regretted by the Employers’
Union, who state, of course, that it has
ushered into Parliamentary life a number
of men whose chief desire is the salary
attached to the position of M.L.A. This
is even less true than it would be to say
that Premier Parkes’ tenure of office is
prompted solely by a thirst for pay. The
Labour-candidates did not put up/op the £3OO,
but because of it ; had it not existed they could
not have offered their honest services to their
fellows, and if there be one cogent reason for
the introduction of payment of members it is
that affords working men and poor men an
opportunity ot representing men who are also
working-men and also poor men. Of course the
enemies of democracy are prepared to style
these “Representatives of Humanity ” —as
someone designated them during the elections —
presumptuous, unintellectual, uncultured ; but,
judging from what we’ve seen and heard,
should the labour-party move solidly and
quietly, they will, not only as a party but as
individuals, compare favourably in all points
with the other parties and their components —
and more than favorably with the men whom
they have displaced.

popular House “Peter Pickle”; You make a mis'

The mutilated body of Commander Lew-
ington, of the N.S.W. Naval Forces, was found
strewn on the railway-line at Hurstville, near
Sydney, on Saturday night. The matter is
wrapped in mystery. No one knew that the Com-
mander had any reason for travelling in the
direction of Hurstville.
A certain young man whose legitimacy
was questioned stood for Parliament, and, at a
meeting, was asked who and what his father was.
Without moving a muscle, he replied ; “ As it is a
wise child, my friend, who knows his own father,
lam not sure about my father. However, lam
sure my mother is a lady ; she is known as a lady
to all hereabouts. If you dispute this, come up
here.” He was elected.
S.M. Herald prints an article which
means, if it means anything, that in re-distri-
buting the N.S.W. electorates care must be taken
that commercial constituencies shall be created
as against the labour-constituencies. However,
there was nothing the matter with the N.S.W.
constituencies as at present arranged until their
aggregate vote panned out against the Calico-
Jemmies.
Some two years ago the block of laud on
which a certain palatial building stands was
occupied by 12 small red-brick hovels, always in a
sad state of repair. This nice property was not
seldom in the police-reports as “ the lowest part
of Melbourne.” Eleven out of the 12 were
notorious dens of vice. The rout was collected
for years by the man at the corner cottage, and
taken once a fortnight down to the office of—well,
the partners were both stars in the religious and
temperance world-and the same question was
always asked : “ Was he sure there were no bad
characters living in the row?” The man knew
his cards and answered: “No, certainly not.”
And so the farce went on until the row was con-
demned, and at boom-time was sold for a small
fortune. Of such is the Kingdom of Heaven.
There is little chance of any Licensed
Victuallers’ Association going bung.

Our recent visitor, young Earl Dudley, is
