On Monday night the N.S.W. labour-party
had captured 18 Ministerial seats and had only
taken four Protectionist seats. Included in the
18 were 16 of the most servile and useful votes
that any government ever had. Parkes could
have counted on them to lynch the Queen with-
out a trial. The labour-party have now also
annexed the two new seats at Hartley and New-
town.
Rabbi Davis, as the head ecclesiastic of
(we believe) longest standing in N.S.W., should
have religious precedence at Government House,
Sydney. We would advise Governor Jersey to
adopt this suggestion in the interest of future
Governors who may not be so well off as the head
of Child’s bank. We have already seen at least
one N.S.W. Governor who would have been glad
of the influence of the Rabbi.
The Bulletin has always felt that the
plural vote was a great force, but it never, until
the present N.S.W. general election, had the
slightest idea of how great that force was. Look
at the case of Central Cumberland. For that out-
side constituency there was a polling-place in the
city at which nearly one thousand votes were
recorded ! In other words, nearly a thousand
allotments cast their weight against the flesh and
blood of resident voters.
United labour has shown its strength at
the N.S. W. general elections by returning up to
date 24 candidates out of the 86 elected. If such
things be done in the green leaf what may not be
done in the dry ? One has to consider not only
the weakening of the monopolistic side by the
abolition of plural voting but also the strengthen-
ing of the workers by the addition, under a system
of registration of voters, of perhaps 40,000 or
50,000 enfranchised nomad electors.
At Melbourne, a young “ praste ” in
embryo, who has not yet completed his appren-
ticeship, and who owns much coin, which was
ultimately destined for the Church, has thrown
oyer divinity for the Divine, and announced
his intention of following Sara to the end of the
earth. The Pope’s local representative for-
warded him a bull, a pastoral, and an encyclioal
in quick succession, which he converted into fuel.
The Church seems at present to stand a very fair
; show of “ getting left.”
The Australian “ Plimsoll-mark ” is a thing
to be scoffed at. It is a mere whim—a variable
line rising or falling according to the caprice of
ship-owners and the helplessness or careless in-
difference of crews. It is a fact well-known to
the present writer from personal observation that
colliers come constantly to Sydney with their
bulwarks but a few inches above water, and that
they almost invariably prefer to sneak up the
harbour at night. Not many days ago, one such
collier, loaded chin-deep in a choppy sea, broke
her steering-gear, and had a miraculous escape
from foundering oft Botany Heads.
The higher education of woman, writes a
nasty, wicked Melbourne correspondent, is likely
to receive a fresh impetus at the ball to be given
by the Austral Salon, where the followers of the
cult of Aspasia will have an opportunity of im-
proving on the lesson in dress, taught at their late
amateur trouble. The ball will be plain and
fancy—that is, the women will be plain, and the
greater portion of the dress will be left to the
fancy of the spectator, since it seems that the
primary step in the elevation of women will be
to elevate her petticoats as far above the ankles as
possible.
From an English paper :
A most pathetic little scene was enacted the other day
in the Guardians’ committee-room at Huddersfield, of all
places in the world. A woman, evidently in the depth of
poverty—she looked as if she had never known what it was
to have enough to eat—entered the room and presented the
Guardians with fifty pounds. The intense delight and
pride on the woman’s face as she did so, was beyond
description. Years ago, it seems, her father had fallen
into evil days, and been obliged to accept parish relief.
The woman had calculated that, in all, he had received
fifty pounds, and she worked night and day to save the
money, and thus remove the stain of pauperdom from her
father’s memory. Think of the misery, the pinching, the
starving, that £5O represents; for the donor is a char-
woman, who rarely earns more than eight shillings a
week. The Guardians were most unwilling to accept the
money, but the woman insisted upon doing so ; for she
could not rest, she said, so long as the money remained
unpaid.
The cable humbug states that Sir W.
Gordon-0 umming has been joyously received by
his tenantry, who are unanimously convinced of
his innocence. This looks a nice old feudal item,
but the average agricultural tenant is so utterly
bankrupt that his one hope lies in getting his rent
reduced, and how much reduction would have
been granted to the tenant who didn’t unanim-
ously believe Sir W. Gordon-Oumming to bo
innocent? Not much, we should bet, for even if
Gumming be not a card-sharper, he is a man with
two surnames joined together by a hyphen, and an
individual like that is capable of anything. And, in
spite of that, it is three chances to one that it was
only the tenants who gathered together and cheered
who were “unanimous,” and the tenants who
stayed away weren’t unanimous at all. Also it
doesn’t matter a cent in any case, and the whole
affair doesn’t amount to any more than a dog-
fight.
London Hawk strongly advises hostesses
to keep out of their houses males of this type ;
Generally young, or aping juvenility, elaborately over-
dressed, with floured faces and flowered coats, nearly
always clean shaved, with pencilled eyebrows, and
greased and scented hair, elaborate lingerie, bejewelled
and gloved hands, bangles and big ties, and always an air
of doing something, playing and composing a little, sing-
ing a little, painting—sometimes themselves—a little,
designing a little, and generally dabbling with any art but
that of self-defence. They generally hang about in
groups, and irritate one to almost hitting them by their
squeaky voices and the kangaroo or squirrel-like fashion
in which they use their little paw-like hands. The few
poor women they associate with they always address in a
Platonic sort of way, and speak of them as “such n
dear,” or “ quit e a sweet person,” but generally they have
lit tic good-feeling for women, and occasionally speak of
them as if they owed them a personal spite. This class of
social horror is never heard to use strong language,
and lias an unnatural objection to a coarse story or a
sailor-like expression, and his name is seldom associated
with that of a woman in a scandal. lie is generally
detested by men who were disgusted at his finnikin ways,
his falsetto voice, his ring-scrawled hands, and the indes-
cribable something which makes manly men’s flesh creep
and feel an inclination to kick something very hard.
This kind of man may also be seen in Australia.
Davitt is disgusted because Parnell collars
the Irish Labor Party. •
Feeling among the Irish-Australians and
Irishmen in Australasia appears to strongly
favour the retention of Parnell as the Irish leader.
The Auckland (N.Z.) Catholic Literary and De-
bating Society the other night decided in his
favour by 16 votes to 11.
Sara Bernhardt evinces a respect for talent
in other walks than drama and art, Mr. Joe
Choynski, the victor of Dooley, having been her
guest on a recent Sunday. By the way, Choynski,
who is making friends everywhere he goes, is, we
hear, a nephew of the Jewish Rabbi at Ballarat.
Advt. from Mauritius Moniteur :
A STAMP COLLECTOR, the possessor of a collection,
of 12,541 stamps, wishes to marry a lady who is
an ardent collector and the possessor of the blue
penny stamp of Mauritius, issued in 1847.
That stamp, by the way, is said to be worth some
hundreds of pounds.
A certain N.S.W. parson just “up the
line ” from Sydney coin plains that he is called by
his people “at peculiar hours of the night” to
celebrate marriages. Out of nearly 120 marriages
at which he has officiated in the town, 29 took
place between midnight and six o’clock in the
morning.
There are eight single-taxers among the
N.S.W. Labour members. However, it must be
remembered that they have been elected on the
labour and not on the pure single-tax ticket.
Single-taxers are nothing if not enthusiastic, and
it is reasonable to suppose that each member
polled every single-tax vote in his constituency.
The statement that a live Australian
Governor is to be a witness in a double-barrelled
divorce-case, to be heard in England, refers to
Gov. Carington, who “unwittingly afforded the
hospitality of Government House, Sydney, to an
erring couple on tour.” You all know the lady—
she sings like a hir’d.
Telegram from Barcaldine (Q.) in Brisbane
Telegraph :
This (June 18) being the anniversary of the battle of
Waterloo, the townspeople are entertaining the military
at a grand ball this evening.
The Bulletin has no doubt that the Tozers and
Ricardos and sich were quite good enough Wel-
lingtons for the local Duchesses of Richmond.
It was last week cabled that ex-Lord
Mayor Isaacs, of London, had resigned his alder-
manship owing to having been connected with
certain “unfortunate” companies during his
mayoral terra. One of these was the Anglo-
Austrian Printing and Publishing Union, which,
after getting £93,000 out of the public with which
no trace of business of any kind had been done,
wound up with a balance of 25 guineas on its
books.
A Melbourne caterer has a quarrel with a
big Southern corporation, which, during the strike,
hired him to feed blacklegs, and, so he says, told
him to spare no expense. Taking them at their
word, he stoked the “free ” labourers with turkey
and truffles ; now the employers refuse to pay on
the stuffed-turkey scale. “ Men may laugh and
riot till the feast is o’er ; then comes the reckoning
and they laugh no more.”
In 1 homas C. O’Mara, barrister-at-law,
whe died of consumption at Burwood, Sydney, on
Tuesday morning last, Australia has lost one of
her brightest, most eloquent and most loveable
sons. When once Mr. O’Mara took up a question
in earnest no one who has ever stood on our soil
could be more eloquent with tongue or pen. That
nervous disposition which is so often allied to
quick perception and brilliant mental gifts was
physically his curse.
In Melbourne Public library are some
rough carven idols (from New Ireland). On a
certain reverend evangelist seeing them he rushed
straight to the trustees, and at last, to stop his
tongue, the idols were put in frocks, much to the
amusement of visitors. Last week three small
boys were seen down on their knees trying to
unhitch the frocks and see what sort of knees the
wooden gods were endowed with. This comes of
dressing idols.
The snagging-boats were working near
Hay. The Court was being held. The day, to
say the least of it, was hot, and so was the judge’s
temper. A half-drunken snagger rolled in and,
lifting his hat, saluted his honor with, “Hello,
Charley, how are you?” “Remove that man,”
thundered the judge. When he was being re-
moved the drunk was heard to say: “Damn
queer, can’t speak to one’s own brother but get
lumbered.” That night two men in a hurry took
the track to the river. They looked like police-
men. Next morning the snagging-boats were
miles up the stream.
Rev. Ralph Brown, well-known and
respected in Melbourne, is better known as
“ Jockey Brown the Parson.” When he lived at
Preston he came to town across country, and the
stranger seeing the flying parson would think, by
the appearance of his nag and himself, that he
had been for a doctor. The Rev. R, B. is a combi-
nation of professions, and how he keeps them all
going is wonderful. He saves souls, teaches
shorthand and a system of mnemonics, is a
phrenologist and thought-reader, and acts as
builder and architect to his own denomination.
Some of his spare time is filled in by attending
local option and mothers’ meetings.
A young Pentridge prisoner, under three
years’ sentence, the other day tried to hang him-
self. The visiting P.M. thereupon sentenced him
to twelve months’ additional gaol and fourteen
days’ solitary confinement ! We ask logical people
to consider well this sentence, and then say
whether anything more gratuitously brutal and
useless and senseless can ever have been done by
King Theebaw of Burmah, or King Coffee Oalcali
of Dahomey, or that Maiiipuri potentate who
lately roasted two live prisoners before a slow
fire? What is the Australian medical profession
about that it doesn’t rise up as one man to pro-
test against such insane savagery?

A Murray “ Cod."—Albury as the Federal
Capital.
The political motto of the Labour Party :
“ Support in return for concessions.”
New York Tribune says Australia is
following exactly in the line of the United States.
Parkes’ sudden affection for One-Man-
One-Vote is Circean. Ho affects to love it that he
may destroy it.
Frawncis Abigail’s trade knowledge will
now, we presume, be utilised in the “ Upper ”
House.
Without an exception that The Bulletin
knows of, the new N.S.W. Labour members are
good Republicans.
B. R, Wise champions M‘Millan’s budget.
But he fights shy of allusions to Macmillan's
Magazine.
The “ privilege ” of Australian Property
will, in the near future, be not to exercise plui’ai
votes but to pay plural taxes.
We take the Federal Convention as an
instalment, a kind of sketch, outline, or tracery,
useful in its humble way.
Twenty-five members of the late N.S.W-
Legislative Assembly have voluntarily retired
from political life. Many more have been and
will be hustled out,
A Victorian : “ Glorious is the news of
the N.S.W. elections. The old time comes o’er
us, that of Charles Jardine Don and Richard
Heales.”
The brightest feature of the N.S.W.
general elections is the manner in which the old
sectarian feuds have been sunk by the workers.
Exit the Parkes pig-vote party.
Parkes boasted that his Government would
obtain 48 of the 53 seats contested on the first
day of the N.S.W, general elections. As a matter
of fact, they only secured 23.
()ne of the elect of East Sydney paid £5O
for the composition of his election-speeches.
That he had a lesson to repeat was patent to all
who heard his parrot-like utterances.
We notice in the suddenly-awakened
Sydney daily papers that “Labour is King.”
Now. Labour is not King, but he is going to be
President of the United Australian Republic.
“ The Australian Federation Conference,”
remarks an English paper, “adopted the word
Commonwealth perhaps with an eye to a Lord
Protector in the shape of Sir Henry Parkes.”
More than one candidate at the N.S.W-
general election is that sort of Englishman, who*
considering the general views of this island’s
population, could not conscientiously represent
any Australian constituency.
Paris correspondent of the Times says
that there are five million Jews in Russia, who,
with the capacity for increase that characterises
the race, may grow to exceed, in forty years, the
orthodox population of Russia.
Glen Innes (N.S.W.) gaol, which cost
£11,300, probably loan-money, is utterly useless.
It was built as the result of log-rolling on the part
of local residents. It has not been used, and is
rotting away in the charge of a caretaker.
“Cocky” Burns, Parkes’ ex-Treasurer’
rejected by St. Leonards, was for nearly 30 con-
secutive years a member of the N.S.W. Legislative
Assembly. His political death is chiefly ascrib-
able to the fact that he dared oppose one-man-one-
vote.
Of course Australia doesn’t trouble itsel f
much -about the Anglo-Italian alliance, but i fc
would like to know what is the immoral considera-
tion given by Italy for such a guarantee as that
the British Mediterranean Fleet is to defend the
Italian ports?

wealth’ is a good—a very good—mouthful, but it
is not a xvord that commends itself much to

than a Monarchy.’ ”
Parkes will offer the N.S.W. labour-
members their political price, but he will tender
it in promissory-notes. If they demand cash, he will
offer to pay them in counterfeit money. There are
m the world some persons with whom it is danger-
ous to do business at all.
Seven would-be legislators dropped their
deposits at the Tasmanian general elections. For
instance, the four candidates for West Devon (one
seat) polled thus :—M‘Call, 531; Aieock, 88;
Button, 67; Fogg, 51. So far, six N.S. Wales
candidates are similarly situated.
Parkes has told a Melbourne interviewer
that the N.S.W. Labour Party is “quite a sur-
prise to him.” He thinks, however, that “it will
be impossible for them to act together in the work
of legislation as a body.” How then did the
Parkes party act together as a body?
“ Sir Henry Parkes went on to say that ho
viewed the victories of the N.S.W. labour-candi-
dates with great pleasure.” Then why did he and
bis Government oppose their candidature? The
labour-party recognise slime when they see it
dribbling from the mouth of the snake.
The Prahran (Melb.)branch of the A.N.A.
has chosen a major in the Mounted Rifles as the
new president. Majahs at the head of a so-called
national movement! Why, the A.N.A. will end
up by being a standing army, or a secret society
of special constables, sworn to stop everything
save their own tongues.
Owing to the recent election to S.A.
Legislative Council of eight new members, there
is now an absolute majority in that Chamber
favouring Corporation and District Councils
assessing on unimproved land-values. The
Assembly for three years sent the Bill up to the
Council,- each time to be rejected.
On the meeting of N.S.W. Parliament
some member should table an enquiry as to how
much if anything extra over contract-price the
N.S.W. Treasury has to pay for inscribing the,
politically defunct O’Connor’s name on Sydney
General Post-office clock bell. Of this the country
may be quite sure : the inscription was not carved
out of O’Connor’s own .brass.
The Marquis of Salisbury, then Lord
Robert Cecil, writing in the Saturday Review in
61, declared that “Englishmen’ssympathies must
necessarily be with the Southern States ” (in the
American Civil war) “ because the Southerners
were asserting their rights as citizens of sovereign
states.” If Salisbury is alive when an Australian
State or States attempts to secede from our Com-
monwealth, what will lie say then ?
There is at least one of the transportation
Acts of George IV.’s reign unrepealed. If N.S.W.
Government ever follows the Queensland example
and attempt to enforce some of the Georgian
statutes, The Bulletin may take into considera-
tion the advisableness of instituting a case or two
under that particular unrepealed measure. If it
does, there will be trouble, especially as it is in
doubt whether 60 years’ possession of land adverse
to the Crown is a good title.

appointment of Michael Davitt to the British
Labour Commission resolves itself into the asser-
tion that the Queen could not address “a Repub-
lican and a Fenian” as her “trusty and well-
beloved ”! Which is simply an argument not for

FISHMONGER: “I say; whistle your dog.”
OWNER OF DOG: “Ugh, what? You’d better whistle your crab.”
shutting out the brave and pure Davitt, but for
shutting down upon a lying form of words which
is a relic of Bastard Bill and his “trusty and

Melbourne Age remarks, in re the Labour
successes at the polls, that the N.S.W. people
have determined to take the control of public
affairs into their own hands, and once and forever
end Government by the little squatting and im-
porting ring. “ Young Australia,” it adds, “ has
come to the front, and has supplanted in power
the imported population, who have been so easily
misled by appeals to insular prejudices, and who
have been indifferent to the politics of their
adopted country.”
As The Bulletin predicted, the Labour
candidates unnecessarily nominated for the
already democratically-represented South Sydney
constituency, spoilt the election in the democratic
interest. They occupied places at the bottom of
the poll—between 900 and 1100 votes behind the
number recorded for Traill, who was returned at
the top, B. R. Wise being second and well up.
The verdict of the electors thus coincides with
that of this paper, given in advance. The ill-
judged action of the Labour Party has lost for the
present to the democratic interest the services of
that able young lawyer, Walter Edmunds.
“ Inasmuch as,” says the S.E. Chronicle,
“the Australian Commonwealth has followed in
a general way the lines of the Constitution of the
United States, it is reasonable to suppose that the
separation from Great Britain will be followed
by the adoption of a Republican form of govern-
ment. No reason is apparent why it should not
succeed as well in Australasia as in America, and
the dawn of the coming century may very likely
see the Australian Commonwealth a Republic—
free, independent and indivisible. Australia has
been in leading strings so long her people do not
realise how strong they are. Now that she has
struck out for herself she is bound to go.”
The Sydney press is divided in its ideas as
to the fiscal faith of the N.S.W. Labour Party.
One section hails them as Freetraders ; another as
Protectionists. As a matter of fact, the bulk of
them have no belief in cither policy. They argue
that whether wealth bo largely acquired in ex-
change for products externally manufactured, or
created chiefly by internal manufacture, that
neither the so-called Freetrade of N.S. Wales or
the Protection of Victoria can ensure the worker
an equitable division of the results of his labour,
or wipeout the unemployed difficulty by throwing
open national resources and opportunities for pro-
duction to all desirous of using them. That being
so, they intend to maintain an armed neutrality.
Sydney Echo remarks that the written
promises of the N.S.W. labour-members to resign
when called upon to do so by a two-thirds majority
of the labour-committee “are no more binding
than a will, which can be revoked or altered at
any time. These candidates can give a written
promise to some other party to the effect that
they will not resign until they are compelled, and
the later promise will effectually revoke the
earlier one.” The labour-party will no doubt feel
greatly obliged for this, frank outburst of Echo
political morality. It is worthy of the political
regime under which the Echo was educated-
under which cheques secretly marked with pin-
holes to show that they were not meant to be
paid, were issued to the creditors of certain poli-
ticians.
'Nine out of ten of the British comments
on the failure of the Victorian loan draw the
deduction that the “collapse of Protection” was
responsible for the failure, and sundry patriotic
magnates of the Sydney commercial world whose
study of economic literature is confined to West-
garth’s Circular and the Sydney Morning
Herald money-article—the weakest thing of the
kind in all Australasia—have not been slow to
parrot the cry. Not one of the moralists, how-
ever, has had the candour to admit that the
public debt per head in Victoria is £9 per head
less than it is in N.S.W.; or that the true cause
of thecollapse of Australian loans is simply that the
big syndicates have either gone bung or are on
the eve of going. The Baring catastrophe riddled
most of the syndicates to disappearing-point.
The Australasian’s Sydney correspondent
remarks that “Federation is in the air” and is
certain to prove a great “bone of contention.”
The Bulletin doesn’t want to be too critical
about this, as the correspondent is doubtless a
well-tailored and harmless individual, but it is
unwise to have a bone up in the air at any time,
and it devoutly hopes that the great Freetrade
party has tied a string to the end of it so as to
haul it down when it is wanted. Besides, the
metaphor isn’t up to much, for Parkes never
worries about bones —it is meat he is hunting
after. And, anyhow, the proceeding ought to bo
discouraged, for a party that has a bone in the
atmosphere will presently be found depositing
grease on the bright lexicon of youth, or it will
try to dig up the white flower of a blameless life
with the sabre of its sires, or something of the
sort. There should be a law against “ the great
Freetrade party” using figures of speecli—the}'
always come to a disastrous end somehow.
Bruce Smith’s election for the Glebe is
quoted as a victory for the conservative side by the
Sydney daily press. Now, no less than 500 plural
votes were recorded in the Glebe, of which, it
cannot be doubted, 90 per cent, were recorded for
Mr. Smith, who nevertheless after all only suc-
ceeded in beating labour-candidate Houghton by
seven votes ! As to M‘Millan’s victory, what can
be expected in East Sydney, the home of plural
voting? After all, the labour-candidate, working-
stonemason Grantham, polled 2241 single votes
against M’Millan’s 3713, the latter being sup-
ported by all the wealth of Sydney and the entire
daily press, which, sofarasweean see, did noton any
one occasion squarely report asingle utterance from
the labor-candidate’s lips. The Bulletin pre-
dicts that when the plural votes of the clubs, &c.,
shall have been struck off the electoral-roll East
Sydney will return at least one labour-candidate
every time.
The Eight Hours Anniversary Committee,
of Melbourne, have voted £l2O of their last
surplus to the local hospitals.
The Queenscliff lifeboat, it now trans-
pires, is only fit for taking out in very fine weather.
Man the lifeboat! man the lifeboat! For the
ship will sink, alas ! Man the lifeboat! man the
lifeboat, while the sea is smooth as glass.
Give the London and Sydney Tea Co. one trial.
They guarantee satisfaction.
“Granny ” and her “ Echo.”*
Granny, cuddled in her chair,
(So the oracles aver)
Thus her spirit did declare
To the bantling at her beck, O—
“ Sit, my babe, upon my knee
Listen mutely unto me
While I sketch my past to thee.”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ First of all I wish to make
This remark—it’s for your sake —
From my past your pattern take,
If you wish to work a ‘ spec.,’ O
F’ollow me, and, through and through,
Copy everything I do ;
Be, in short, my own cuckoo.”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo,
“ Long before I made a name,
I, desirous of the same,
Hit upon a certain aim,
Kept my conscience well in check, 0—
Since I hit it, night and day,
Cash I’ve made the shortest way.
Child, remember all 1 say.”
“ Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ What is life? Its full amount
Is not worth a printer’s fount
If you have no bank-account—
Cannot sit and draw your cheque, O.
Home this lesson I would bring ;
Sling your faith—your honour sling—
Money— money’s everything !”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ Knowing this I’ve kept in sight
Money morning, noon and night—
Neither swerving left nor right,
Brazen face and stiffened neck, O.
With a heart as hard as flint
Coin I’ve got by every dint—
Baby, will you take the hint?”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ To improve my pocket’s health
I’ve been found by steps of stealth
F’awniug at the feet of Wealth
Like a filthy-featured gecko.J
Child, remember when you’re big,
Toady even w retched ‘ prig ’
If he have a broad-cloth ‘ rig.’ ”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ Knowing how Religion pays,
I have walked in ‘ pious ’ ways,
Like a Pecksniff all my days—
Snowy choker round my neck, O.
Baby, now a sprouting root,
If you wish to come to fruit,
Don’t forget a ‘ godly ’ suit.”
“Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ Year by year I laboured hard
Social progress to retard—
If it didn’t suit my card
World and all might go to wreck, O
Still if any progress came,
1 to kudos laid a claim—
Bantling, you must do the same.”
“ Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ Men of genius, what are they?
Scholars ?—do their writings pay ?
These are rubbish, in the way,
Names of no use on a cheque, O !
Chicken, if you see about
Clever fellow on the scout,
Try like me to snuff hiin out.”
“ Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.
“ Here I finish ! Wipe your nose.
Never tread on Mammon’s toes,
Hang your conscience ! —keep your clothes,
Parson-patterned, free of speck, O.
If you wish success to find
Ail that I have thus consigned
Carry, baby, in your mind.”
“ Cuckoo, cuckoo,” quoth the Echo.

“ unnatural offender,” intend strongly to oppose
his threatened expulsion from the House of
Commons. They can’t give up such an ardent
Unionist without a struggle, although a gentleman
who is preaching the Word around Spain, disguised
in a wig and false nose, doesn’t seem of- much
practical use to his party, any way.
Patrick Lenehan Feeney got drunk on a
recent night and was still so unsober on arising to
go to work that he put on his trousers back-to-
front. While ascending the ladder with a load of
bricks he fell, and great was the fall thereof.
Pat’s comrades surrounded him and asked with
sympathy in their voices, “ Are yez hurt ? ”
Phadrig, catching sight of the position of his
unmentionables, replied in feeble tones: “No
bhoys, but oi’ve got a divil of a twisht.”
An emperor doesn’t look well when he
is upside-down. The other day the German boss
potentate was about Mi conduct a review from the
deck of a torpedo-boat, the troops being ranged
along the river bank. He fixed himself in a
haughty and noble attitude, with one leg slightly
in advance of the other, and his breast-bone
thrown out to such an extent that it looked as if
he had a bag of coal inside his waistcoat, and his
sword held high up in the air above his head.
Then the boat gave a heave, and as he was already
tottering and nearly bursting with the tre-
mendous strain of his kingly attitude, that
finished him. His legs flew out like an enraged
fowl and his sword went somewhere twenty yards
away, and he clutched the air with both hands
and vanished into the bottom of the boat. They
found him there swearing violently and with his
helmet smashed down over his eyes, and lifted
him up, for his military pants were so tight that
he couldn’t I'ise without assistance. Then his
helmet was put straight, and somebody lent him
another sword, his own being at the bottom of the
river, and he resumed the attitude of majestic
approbation as before.

Personal Items.
Dibbs has 15 children.
Prince of Wales’ motto —“ I deal.”
Reynolds quotes the N.S.W. poet Holds-
worth—Joe.
“Monsieur Judas” is Fergus Hume’s
latest shocker.
Birmingham Post declares Wales’ deficit
at half a million.
Last week, in Victoria, Sara Bernhardt
went shooting in trousers.
Purves, Q.C., is going to Europe, where he
studied some time-'-in Berlin.
A Melbourne paper avets that Lady Jersey
has a volume of verse on the way.
Gov. Hopetoun, it is stated, has a large
interest in an irrigation block at Renmark colony.
“ I bebeve in trades-unions —I belong to a
very good one myself,” says barrister G. H. Reid.
Lord Blank, one of the Boltou-Park gang,
is teaching music in Melbourne under an assumed
name.
Moorhouse’s bid for the York Arch-
bishopric was a new book on the Apostolic Age.
No go.
Disraeli said : “ When I cannot recall who
a person is that speaks to me, I ask how’s the old
complaint ?”
Sir George Grey’s niece, Mrs. Seymour
George, is remarkably like her distinguished rela-
tive in feature.
One year has elapsed since Shirley Baker
was ejected from Tonga. Why doesn’t he come
round and lecture ?
Parkes’ projected autobiography (if true
to the original) will be a haughty biography and a
naughty biography.
Dr. “Diamond” Beaney, who has been
long ill, is being attended by four doctors, and is
not expected to live.
That accomplished journalist, Mr. Fred.
Broomfield, has just been married to a young
French lady of Sydney.
Sir Daniel Cooper’s stamp collection was
sold to M. Ferrari for £3OOO, and the Galliera
collection, Paris, has cost £57,600.
Composer Luscombe Searelle has a brother
practising as an architect in Melbourne. Both
have to draw houses to make a living.
So Beenthirst's good old dad is sent to
Coventry over the Baccarat biz ! Well, well,
what a thing it is to have a prodigal father !
“To my son George I leave the Island of
Rum ’’—Slab from will of John Bullough, of
Meggernie Castle, Perthshire, lately deceased.
One of the rejected candidates for a
N.S.W. Metropolitan constituency, stood his trial
for murder, many years ago, and was acquitted.
The Prince of Wales suffers from varicose
veins which prevent him from standing long, but
Court journals absurdly write it down influenza.
Selwyn, first Bishop of N.Z., and “ Jack ’
Selwyn, his son, present Bishop of Melanesia,
both rowed for Cambridge in the ’Varsity boat-
race.
“ There was never yet an Irishman,” say*
the London correspondent of the Australasian.
“who was not ashamed of his brogue,” Is it
true?
The graceful lady - writer known as
“A.L.0.E.” (a lady of England) is sister of
Booth-Tucker, General Booth’s son-in-law, and
lives in India.
Frederick Harrison has learnt bullock-
driverese. In the Fortnightly for April he
writes ; “ Mr. Knowles is only playing the larrikin
in his own Review.
Cows were uppermost in the discussion.
“Cows give milk,” said Salomons, Q.C., “but
your case only gives whey.” Salomons’ jokes are
all purely Cockney.
A Mrs. Macfaull was Government Printer
of Western Australia in 1848. She almost equalled
S. V. Winter, of Melbourne Evening Herald, as
an adept in “langwidge.”
The Bulletin knows of an official who
makes more in tips than his Treasury pay comes
to, from bringing about the practical exemptior
of certain persons from juries.
Grandfather Wales has sworn off—is only
allowed to play at grab, with the kids, and win
their pennies, like the toothless lion reduced to
prey on mice, in his old age.
One great advantage derived from the
changing of Sandhurst back to Bendigo has been
over-looked. There’s a Lord Sandhurst, but
there’s never likely to be a Lord Bendigo.
Von Moltke died on the night on which he
had emptied at dinner the last bottle of his
favourite Moselle remaining in his cellar. That is
the time to die—when the last bottle is empty*
Sydney election eggs are not so soft after
all. The ripe hen-fruit item which smote reporter
Du Rieu, of the S.M. Herald, the other night,
blackened his eye, thus adding injury to insult.
Sir William Gordon-Cumming comes of a
constitutionally brave and honourable family in
the North of Scotland. His uncle was Roaleyn
Gordon-Cumming, the famous African lion-hunter,
W. H. Handfield, secretary to the Vic-
torian Education Department, an excellent and
just official, at one time well known in the cricket
field, was found dead in his bed one day last week,
aged 60.
H. H. Champion writes that he recently
met Gladstone and “was much struck by the
evidence of rapid decay in his powers.” The next
question to be considered is—how did Champion
strike W.E.G. ?
Henrik Ibsen, the Norwegian poet, has,
judging from his photo., a strong resemblance to
Rev. Dr. Bevan, of Melbourne. In fact, ten minutes
at a smart barber’s would make Henrik the
Doctor’s double.
Dean O’Hea, of Melbourne, has been
created a Monsignor by the Pope. The Pope
seems always to make Monsignors out of ex-
tremely wealthy old gentlemen who are not
averse to “ parting.”
Ex-Town Clerk Fitzgibbon, of Melbourne,
succeeds to £45,000 under the will of his mother-
in-law, the late Mrs. Dawson (widow of Mickey,
of that ilk), who left £250,000, of which £29,000
goes in charity and personal legacies.
Zola has been exploring Sedan, and finds
that Napoleon lll.’s last night there was spent in
a bedroom with a print of the “ Last Judgment.”
Says Zola ; “It is a horrible region, and looks as
if cultivated by the phantoms of the slain.”
T. K. Pater, P.M., the Adelaide beak
with a liver and a heavy-sentence mania, visited
the Adelaide Gaol the other day. He said :
“Why, I.thought I’d filled this place long ago.
There’s room here for half the community !”

success as a Melbourne suburban magistrate. He
used to incubate all day over a leader, and then
he would go to the restaurant and eat two whole
dinners at once. We never knew such a con-
scientious man.

factor” Mackenzie left the Prince of Wales
in desperate straits for ready money. The
executors of that grovelling Scotchman are be-
lieved to hold Tummy’s little bits of paner to the
nominal value of £250,000.

an UMBRELLA STORY.
It was the Barings who had, perforce, to
put the screw on Wales, over Sandringham, and
he raised £300,D00 somewhere, or quieted the
creditors somehow, though Hirsch proved a broken
reed. “My dear fellow, I’ve got poor Ithrael to
look after.” Blood’s thicker than cham.
Lady Hopetoun is still rather delicate,
we fear. She patronised Melbourne Anglican
Cathedral the other Sunday morning, but after
about fifteen minutes of worship her ladyship
drew herself together and hastily got out of it.
Cold tabernacle doesn’t seem to suit her constitu-
tion.
A Melbourne evening paper mentions as
an item of news that Sir Bryan O’Loghlen and
Daniel O’Connell were at school together. This
reminds us indirectly of the remark that John
Haynes made to Henry YIII. when they were
boys together. We can’t remember what the
remark was, but it doesn’t matter.
Governor Hopetoun, of Victoria, judging
from recent English revelations, is a subscriber to
a “British and Foreign Association,” which, for
the sum of half-a-guinea, will issue to members
letters of introduction to other members, in what-
ever country-resident, “which shall secure the
bearer hospitality and social recognition.”
W. J. Henninghara, journalist, auctioneer
and Bohemian, who last month got three years
for something connected with land-transfers, died
last week in Pentridge prison. He was a well-
known figure in old Melbourne ; the early
divorce-suit Annand and Annand in the fifties
knew him well. He started the first evening
paper in Maoriland.
J. A. Northmore, who died in Adelaide
last week, was one of the best-known men in
S. Australia. Although a broker and ex-mer-
chant, he was a born newspaper man, and attracted
much attention by his able articles when the
Protectionist policy of S. Australia was being
formulated. He had a remarkable memory and a
singularly caustic style.
A correspondent takes the trouble to in-
form Pall Mall Gazette that James M‘Kinley,
of Melbourne, “is not a Scotchman, but an Irish-
man.” Now, we have tried to read Melbourne
Punch, run by Mr. M‘Kinley aforesaid, for a
number of years, and have no hesitation in
declaring that if Mr. M'Kinley be not a Scotch-
man, he ought to be one.
“No one,” said Andrew Kelly, offering
thanks to the West Sydney electors for his return
as a labor M.P., “ will ever see Andy Kelly in a
top-hat.” In future soft hats will be known as
“ Andy Kelly’s ” —and the Sydney hatters must
be a slow lot if one of their number fails to
register a slouch felt at the Patents-office as “ The
Andy Kelly ” within an hour of The Bulletin’s
publication.
Here is how Sara Bernhardt plays with
her marvellous constitution. On Saturday she
rehearses “Adrienne Lecouvreur ” in the morn-
ing, acts “Cleopatra” in the afternoon, and
again in the evening; and directly after the per-
formance is off to Lilydale, 20 miles from the
city, where they report her passing through at
four o’clock on Sunday morning, with a party for
the Black Spur!
Moore Dillon, a former N.S.W. Crown
solicitor, was, like John Williams, noted for good
sayings. The then Clerk of the Peace, Mr.
Moore, had lost one of his organs of vision, and
for appearance sake wore an artificial substitute.
Dillon was on his way to Moore’s office one day
when a friend met him and asked where he was
going. “O,” said Dillon, “I am just off to con-
sult the oracle of Adel phi.”
None of the great financial houses of
Rothschilds are believed to be in the impregnable
position they occupied 20 years ago. The
financial ability of the old barons who have died
off is not maintained in their successors, and the
personal expenditure of the family gets more
extravagant as their c6ups become smaller and lews
frequent. Gilbert, in one of his patter songs,
coupled the names of Rothschild and Baring.
Baring’s wealth has vanished.
The present Emperor of Germany attributes
his perennial sore ear and withered hand to his
connection with “ those scrofulous Guelphs,” but,
perhaps, not with absolute justice. His paternal
grandmother, the Empress Augusta, was a grand-
daughter of the Emperor Paul of Russia, a
horrible mass of disease and a congenital lunatic.
So that a taint of scrofula and hereditary dunder-
headedness are all the ills he can fairly lay at the
door of Her Gracious of England.
“Why,” asks an Australian, writing in
Greater Britain, “does Mr. Stead single out
Mr. Parnell and Sir Charles Dilkc, and say ‘ You
are unfit for public life on account of your private
life,’ and pat the politician on the back whom be
calls the G. O. M. of Australia ? I cannot suppose
such favouritism arises from anything else than
ignorance; but such ignorance respecting our
statesmen and the others who form our colonial
Cabinet Councils makes us smile in the colonies.”
Disraeli is generally said to have been very
mean. “ Years ago I was at High Wycombe,”
writes an English newspaper correspondent,
“ and went into a local grocer’s. The proprietor
was in a great rage, and turning round exclaimed:
‘What do you think of that for a member of
Parliament ? See that half cheese ! It has just
been returned from Mr. Disraeli; as he is going
to London he does not need the remaining half of
a cheese I sent him three weeks ago, and he
wishes the value of this piece deducted from his
bill ? ’ ”
General Smidt, the conqueror in the fight
at Majuba Hill, was lately on tour through Natal,
and was invited by the officers of the British
garrison at Pietermaritzburg to dine with them.
He is described as a “plain-featured, portly
Dutch farmer, with long, black beard, slightly
streaked with grey, attired in black morning coat,
greyish ‘ slop ’ trousers, guiltless of straps, elastic-
side boots, a soft felt hat on his head, and in his
mouth a big briarwood pipe, from which issued
volumes of smoke of the pungent character pecu-
liar to Boer tobacco.”
Sir William Gordon-Cumming has been
formally sacked from the British Army, and the
Queen advertises in the Gazette that she has nc
further need for his services. Now, if Camming
has one scrap of energy about him he will reply by
advertising that he has no further use for the
Queen or Wales or any other member of the Royal
Family, and that they are no longer in his em-
ployment, and are not authorised to represent him
in any way, and that he won’t be responsible for
any liabilities they may incur on his behalf unless
authorised by his signature.
In one of his early cases, the audacity
which has always distinguished “ Jack ” Want in
court was displayed most effectively. The case
having been called, the young barrister said, in
the usual way, “let notice be given.” “In what
paper?” was the enquiry of the grey-haired clerk
of the court. Mr. Want mentioned some out-of-
the-way paper. “I don’t know such a paper,”
said the clerk disdainfully. “ Don't interrupt
the court proceedings, Mr. Clerk,” said J.W.,
“ for if you begin to tell us what you don’t know,
there will be no time for anything else.”
Samson Fox, of Leeds, lias become a
millionaire by his invention of corrugated boiler-
flues. Howe, the sewing-machine inventor, de-
rived £lOO,OOO a year from his chief invention, and
the author of Singer’s sewing-machine left a for-
tune of nearly £3,000,000 sterling. The inventor
of india-rubber tips at the end of lead-pencils
made £20,000 out of the scheme, and the man who
invented metal plates for wear as boot-protectors
got a quarter of a million of money out of it. The
patentee of the roller-skate made £200,000, and the
inventor of the common needle-threader derived
an income of £2OOO a year.
Humour and pathos are strangely blent in
the story recently told by a contemporary of how
that volatile young lawyer and politician, W. P.
Crick, of N.S.W., lately received from England a
letter from an old lady who, having read about a
“scene” in the N.S.W. Parliament in which
W. P. C. was mixed up, concluded that he was
identical with her long-lost step-son and proceeded
to tell him of her wretched circumstances—of how
she had four shillings a week to live on, and of how
considerate her poverty-stricken landlord had
been to her. Then she went on to advise William
to shun drink as he would the Evil One, which so
tickled the gay and festive young N.S.W.
attorney (who has no stepmother and never had,
both his parents still being alive) that he sent her
a few pounds to brighten her misery.
The late Madame Blavatsky, at the age of
fifty, married a boy of sixteen (in revenge,
probably, for having, when seventeen, been
married to a man of sixty), who went stark
staring mad on the day after the wedding.
Madame was accustomed to treat this not par-
ticularly gratifying incident precisely as she
treated many other actions of hers. She never
married the boy : it was her “spook” who went
to the altar and drove the poor young husband
out of his wits ! She was a woman of great per-
sonal fascination, an undoubted mesmerist, and
exercised her power with the most absolute want
of scruple. She had her wants provided for her
by people whom she had drawn within the sphere
of her uncanny influence. Young men left their
professions, abandoned their families, threw up
their prospects in life at her bidding.
An incident of Sir George Grey’s vast
meeting at Sydney Centennial Hall, demonstrates
the profound reverence in which the great old
Radical was held by his audience of 6000. Speak-
ing of Abraham Lincoln’s early career in the
American back-blocks, Grey made an unfortunate
mistake by transposing the commencing conso-
nants of two words in the manner so much
affected by youngsters at school. Such a blunder
would have occasioned, from a large section of the
audience at all events, a tremendous burst of
laughter if made by any other public man, but on
this occasion there was not the faintest sign of
merriment, although the slip, a very awkward
one indeed, must have been noticed by everyone
within the range of the speaker’s voice. It was
an eloquent though silent tribute of respect, from
an audience consisting largely of blunt toilers, not
ordinarily very delicate in such matters.
D. 0. P. Moodie, who died on the voyage
to England from the Transvaal awhile ago, was
an old and eccentric Australian pressman. He
came to Australia fresh from hunting experiences
in South Africa, about 20 years ago, and used to
do the Adelaide block in hunting costume. He
started a paper in Adelaide called the Portonian,
but, like its owner, it was never wealthy, and died.
Moodie walked from Adelaide to Melbourne
■years ago to chastise A. T. Clark, of the Williams-
town Advertiser, who had pained Moodie’s loyal
heart by “insulting” Her Gracious. Although
eccentric, Moodie was very popular with every-
body, and his pronounced Bohemian proclivities
and devil-may-care habits made him notorious in
the sacred city. He was most generous with his
money, and would cheerfully spend his last shilling
if it returned a modicum of fun, or enabled him to
do a good turn to any hard-up man.
Of the Labour candidates so far returned
to the N.S. Wales Legislative Assembly, three,
Johnson, Scott, and M'Gowan, are boilermakers,
the latter having been discharged from the
Government workshops after his nomination ; two
are ex-seamen, Clark and Davis, the latter being
secretary to the Seamen’s Union ; one, Murphy, is
secretary marine officers; Darnley is a plasterer ;
Bavister, a bricklayer; Danahey, an engineer;
one, Hollis, a doctor; three are miners, Began,
Cook, and Cann, the latter (president of the Amal
gamated Miners’ Association) being returned un-
opposed for Sturt; one, Newman, is a mine
manager ; Clark (St. Leonards) is an auctioneer;
three are compositors, Sharp, Fitzgerald, and
Houghton, the latter being also secretary to the
Trades and Labour Council; Hindle is, or was, a
shop employe; Kelly is secretary to the Wharf
Labourers’ Union ; Cotton, an ex-shearer, runs
the Democrat newspaper, has an interest in a
Newtown spoke-factory, and may be classed, like
Black, as a journalist.

The Bulletin rises to enquire what has
become of Lord and Lady Carington of late.
Lady C., from all we hear, appears to have
dropped correspondence completely with Potts
Point, and even Lord Protector Parkes doesn’t
get his “few lines” from the Baron every mail
now as was his wont. The'Caringtons appear to
have pretty well ended their friendship with all
sorts of N.S.W. people. In view of the fact of
the reduction of passenger-rates “home ” and the
number of nonentities who make for Yurrup and
dear old Hingland by every mail-steamer, the
Caringtons have done a wise thing in their own
generation—and the next one—in giving the conge
to their Australian acquaintances. The Baron
came to N.S.W. to allow of his finances being put
on a firmer footing, and now that this has been
accomplished he can see fresh breakers ahead if he
keeps open his old grey bouse for all Australians.
The Bulletin will give a bound copy of its “Pro-
verbs ” to the first Australian who finds the Baron
at home, after this date, when he calls at Wycombe
Abbey.
« • •
“ It has been authoritatively denied that
the Prince of Wales was concerned in the election
of Mr. Arthur Wilson, one of the witnesses in the
late baccarat case, as a member of the Marl-
borough Club,” says a London cable. This, on
the face of it, is sheer nonsense, the fact being
that no one can possibly be elected to the Marl-
borough Club without the personal concurrence of
the Prince of Wales, who has a veto on all nomina-
tions just in the same way as Court etiquette
gives him a veto with respect to the list of guests
invited to meet him at any private house. To be
admitted to the Marlborough Club means that
you have been chosen as a member or “ hanger-
on ” of the Prince of Wales’ own special social
coterie, and no man has ever been admitted with-
out Tummy’s express sanction.
* # #
The Marlborough, says a London paper,
started by the Prince of Wales and certain
dissatisfied members of White’s when their com
mittee decided to prohibit smoking except in
certain rooms, is about the most exclusively Royal
club in London, so much so that a correspondent
informs the London cox-respondent of the Man-
chester Guardian he had to rise from his chair
at the luncheon-table and stand at attention no
less than five times in half-an-hour, as this or that
princeling made entrance. The election of every
member bears the Prince of Wales’ vise, and the
latest to pass the ordeal (prior to Arthur Wilson)
was Henry Irving.
# * *
The Victorian artists’ reception to Bern-
hardt, at Melbourne Athenaeum, was about as
funny as they make such things. The function
had been arranged to last from 3 to 4.30 p.m.,
but Sara conveniently misunderstood the invitation
to mean that she was expected to come in at the
death. So she arrived in a great hurry, just as
the shades of evening were beginning to fall, and
in about ten minutes it was time for her to go.
Meanwhile Sara had duly waxed ecstatic over
some samples of local art, and heard an elegant
address in French from the hon. sec. to the effect
that she was being welcomed as a painter and
sculptor, no less than as a divine artist on the
stage. Then the gifted victim smiled graciously
at a few people and rushed away. Another of the
penalties of greatness had been paid.
* * *
Sir William Clarke “ shouted ” Sara Bern-
hardt’s shooting picnic the other Sunday to Mon-
day. By the way, although the Hebrews have gi ven
fair support to the French plays, they seem afraid
to make any special demonstration in Sara’s
honour. Which perhaps is just as well, for the
difference between her and a mob of bookmakers’
wives would be very marked at any special
Jewish gathering.

“ Some artist,” says London Hawk,
“ appears to have been hoaxing the editor of
Scribner. The frontispiece to this month’s issue
shows Sir Henry Parkes, the Premier of N.S.W.,
and his wife crossing Broadway, New York,
and a realistic touch is given to the picture
by the addition of a pet pug. As it happens. Sir
Henry never set foot in New York in his life.”
The Hawk is wrong in the latter statement. But
the present Lady Parkes never set foot in N.Y.,
and we are not at all certain that the late Lady P.
ever did so. The lady who crossed Broadway may
be Stella, of whom the poet Parkes sings :
And on the Rocky Mountain’s height,
Eight thousand feet above the sea, love,
My Stella kissed me through the night,
Yes, darling, thou wert with me, love.

A Bulletin correspondent pertinently
enquires how it is that a clergyman who has
admittedly appropriated £GOO, the trust funds of
two children, escapes the criminal law, while a
bank-clerk, who also takes other people’s money in
the hope of replacing it after pocketing the surplus
made by it, gets years of imprisonment?
The Victorian branch of the British
Medical Association has just discussed the ques-
tion of admitting lady-doctors as members—this,
wc presume, in view of the fact seven or
eight lady-graduates of Melbourne I niversity
will next year commence practice in Victoria.
Dr. Cobb held that ladies should not bo admitted,
as either the free discussion of all classes of
medical subjects would become impossible or else
the chivalrous feelings that ought always be
entertained towards women would suffer. Lady-
doctors will, The Bulletin thinks, do excellent
business, especially in regard to the smaller
maladies of women and children, and their
services will be greatly in request by jealous hus-
bands and by old maids who don’t know what s
the matter with them. It will be a long time,
however, ere female practitioners gain the confi-
dence of their sex to the same extent as that in
which it is possessed by the male sawboneses.
Lovely woman looks to man as her natural pro-
tector, and we have yet to meet the woman who
has any confidence in the other woman. The
world may argue all round this fact as it likes,
but the fact remains all the same.
# * *
Dr. Cobb’s delicacy about arguing certain
medical subjects before woman goes in the direc-
tion of proving, if any proof were wanted, ttiat
men are really a deal more modest than women,
and that they are in the habit of over-rating the
delicacy of the fair sex under such circumstances.
A very little hospital experience will teach any-
body that.
* * *
In spite of a little delicate shriek from
one Dr. Cobb, writes a Bulletin correspondent,
the Victorian branch of the British Medical
Association has decided to admit duly qualified
ladies to membership. It is tolerably obvious
that a determination to boycott women-doctors
would have very shortly resulted in their forming
their own association, but this contingency was
not hinted at by the broken-up monopolists who
so magnanimously accepted the inevitable last
week. They used the occasion as another oppor-
tunity to trot out the old professional chestnut
that they are engaged in a holy mission, and the
meeting smiled a gentle, kerristian smile at the
speaker who said—“ When women showed an
earnest desire to contribute their share in the
alleviation of suffering humanity it was not their
(the men’s) function to discourage them.” The
time is going by when these qualified extortioners
may expect the most ignorant readers to swallow
such gammon. That women have been alleviating
suffering, ever Adam was first laid up with
indigestion and Eve made him a mustard-plaster,
is as certain as the fact that the average
doctor works to alleviate his qwn wants, and
is no more of a philanthropist than his twin
brother, the lawyer. The one great aim of the
average doctor is to be able to charge fees so high
that the bulk of suffering humanity (which is
extremely hard-up) must die rather than call him
in, and when he is called in to see a squatter who
has nothing the matter with him, he gets hold of
the first woman he sees and recommends “ careful
nursing.” Thirty guineas. The doctors’ gratuitous
work in the hospital is absolutely necessary to
their business. In the first instance they must
learn how to make a decent pretence of knowing
something, and when their private practice is
established they actually fight for a position as
visiting slaughterers because the advt. is mighty
cheap at the price. Yet they would have us
believe that they labour for pure love of God’s
creature, in a city of high fees and horseracing,
where the death-rate always rises on Cup Day
because every owner of a large brass-plate is punt-
ing down at Elemington. How many doctors are
there who will leave a critical case and rush off to
the races, quite indifferent to the probability of
the patient suffering a relapse during the after-
noon ? They all do it in this country. Not that
they are colder-blooded than others of their kind
in other countries. The real trouble is that they
make too much money, the result of that lack of
wholesome competition which women are prepar-
ing to supply. And the excuse for their apparent
callousness is the knowledge that a male M.D.
jerking out his commonplaces at a shilling a word
is often not of the slightest practical use in a sick
room after his first visit. The responsibility rests
with the women watchers, who send rouml again
and again for the doctor only to hear him say
that they are doing everything possible, and it all
depends on the nursing.

MORE GAIETY.
Back again are the Gaiety Girls,
With scarlet lips and teeth like pearls,
And many a smart new cockney wile,
And costumes built in an airy style—
Embodiments bright of arts that please
In a different line to Sara B.’s ;
The wheel of Time, with its constant whirls,
Has brought us back the Gaiety Girls.
But the Gaiety “boys”—O, where are they?
Faded and gone, they’ve had their day.
Where are the smart young tailors’ blocks
Who swelled, of yore, in a private box ?
Where are the gorgeous “ Silver Kings ”
Who gave the bouquets and diamond rings,
And suppers—for smiles and such like things'
Ask of the winds that cool their brows,
As they shear the sheep or milk the cows ;
Ask of the friends who lent them cash
In those land-boom times when friends were rash ;
“ Ask a policeman ” —he might know
The mashers of three short years ago.
The Gaiety Girls wear gems that gleam,
And life for them is a lively dream,
And whether their names be old or new
They look the same at a passing view.
There’s a Gaiety dash in their dance and walk,
And a Gaiety turn in their mode of talk,
A recognised style of form and face—
But the Gaiety “ boys,” they change apace.
Sweet is the sound of the syrens’ song
To the boys of to-day, who will soon go wrong,
Though sad is the sound of the song when sung
To the stone-broke ghosts of the boys gone bung.
BALLAD OF A BEARD.
Once there was an Karl,
Small and pale and weird,
Whose supremest wish
Was to grow a beard;
But his Countess, she
Would not hear of this—
Horrid hair she could
Ne’er abide to kiss.
Then she journeyed “ home"
And he cried with glee—
“ Folks will pretty soon
See what they shall see."
So, forsev’ral weeks.
He remained perdu,
Then, one gorgeous day,
Blossomed into view,
And the people saw,
With emotion strong,
He had grown a beard
Quite three inches long I
She returned, and he,
Sorry for his rash
Act, cut off his heard,
Leaving his moustache ;
But the Countess, she,
With a haughty scoff
Pointing to h's mo.,
Muttered •* Shave it off 1”
He obeyed, of course,
“Splendid joke! Ha! ha!"
Now he is laid up
With Neuralg-i-a.
Fact.
IF BURDEKIN WERE KNIGHTED.
What words could mortal pen employ,
in prose or flowing measure,
To tell our transcendental joy,
Our gratitude and pleasure —
What adjectives could writers find,
What metaphors excited,
To represent our state of. mind
if Burdekin were knighted ?
Ye gods ! imagine, if ye can
(Although the job’s not easy),
Our pride in that majestic man,
So affable and greasy ;
We worship him as “ Mister” plain—
We’d be so dashed delighted
Our minds would hardly bear the strain,
If Burdekin were knighted.
The dogs of sunny New South Wales
Would bark their exultation,
The midnight cats with stand-up tails
Would typify the nation;
On each high hill throughout the land
A beacon would be lighted
To let the heavens understand
That Burdekin was knighted.
Nor east nor west no Mayor deserves
His Sovereign’s reward more ;
From grovel’s paths he never swerves,
He couldn't love the Lord more.
Her royal trumpet he has blown,
I ler noble deeds recited :
V. R. would need a bigger throne
Jf Burdekin were knighted.
How long must New South Walers kneel
And pray some Tory part}'
To bid us kill the fatted veal
And chortle “Jubilate !”?
How long ere years of loyal tee
Are loyally requited,
And tidings come across the sea
That Burdekin is knighted ?

( There is a row going on between the S.A.
Government, the Acting-Chief Justice and
Governor Kintore about the appointment of a
Queen’s Counsel. The Governor, on the recom-
mendation of the Chief Justice, wants to appoint
H. E. Downer, M.P., but the Government won’t
listen to any but the claims of E. P. Nesbit, a
clever young counsel, for the position. None of
the parties will give way, and there the matter
will remain until Chief Justice Way returns.

* * #
The Australian Mutual Provident Society
last week got leave from N.S.W. Supreme Court
to refrain from paying up the £lOOO life-policy of
Mr. J. O. S. Evans, deceased (it denied that he
had ever completed his policy), who had, after
being treated for dropsy following on kidney-
disease, declared to the society that he had only
consulted a doctor for rheumatism •within the pre-
vious five years. Now the A.M.P. is generally
most careful in its medical examinations. How
on earth could any A.M.P. doctor ever have
passed a man in such a condition? The Bulle-
tin’s idea about life-assurance is that policies
should be absolutely indefeasible-that the com-
panies should allow in their rates for all risks from
fraud or misrepresentation of any kind, holding
their medical officers, and not the relatives of the
deceased, responsible when such a case as that of
Mvans crops up. We do not deny that under
existing conddions, the A.M.P. was quite justified
in disputing the Evans claim.

Sydney Evening News lately complained
that it had “suffered much from poetasters.” By
the way, did the News ever hear of an enter-
prising Sydney newspaper proprietor who once
issued a circular informing bush bards that poetry
would be accepted and inserted at advertising-
rates ?
* * #
Why are Australian solicitors, who stick
to their clients’money, not punished for theft as
well as struck off the rolls? When a baker’s
collector embezzles his employer’s money, he is not
only struck off the rolls but gets six months’ hard.
We pause for a reply, and will feel very tired
before we get a satisfactory one.
* * *
The odium theologicum, it would seem, is not
dead, but only in quarantine. A North Sydney
Presbyterian parson of the old school was “ verra
displeased ” with a respected memberof his flock for
nominating a Parliamentary candidate of another
faith, and reasoned with him. The reply
was forcible and unanswerable : “ My dear sir, I
thought I couldn’t go far wrong in nominating for
Parliament a man whom you chose to open your
last church bazaar.”
* * *
Tincture of ginger is the latest fashionable
liquor in high-toned British society. It is used
chiefly by ladies, the female sex having grown
tired of that dreary muddlement which is pro-
duced by sal-volatile and eau-de-cologne. The
peculiarity of tincture of ginger is that it con-
tains 95 per cent, of alcohol, and is calcu-
lated to eat holes in a rhinoceros. This
sort of thing makes the teetotal movement look
unpromising. Teetotalism has been toiling at the
pump for about a century without abolishing one
single liquor among all the innumerable drinks
that are raging throughout the globe ; and mean-
while the number of new varieties of grog are
past counting.

Judge Windeyer, who ought to know, says
that groat mischief is done by the law refusing
divo - ce for desertion. The Judge is obviously
right. I)c facto desertion is in itself divorce.
Ladies whose complexions consist chiefly
of bismuth should keep clear of the electric-light
—or else they should buy new complexions.
Bismuth looks all very well under gas, but when
the wearer comes under the electric jet, her
countenance sends out a sudden glare just like
powdered glass, or like the glitter of a mundic
reef in a strong light, or like the outside of a
kerosene tin, and then she isn’t nice to look at.
Several ladies we know might kindly make a note
of this.

Queensland history sometimes repeats it-
self elsewhere: —“Therehasbeen alittle Bar quarrel
in Wales. A father and his son were members of a
Welsh circuit. The father was made judge, and
the son secured a large and lucrative practice in
his father's Court. The Bar grumbled, and did
nothing; but when the second son was in due
time called to the Bar and proposed to join the
circuit, the Bar mess refused to elect him. The
judge and the eldest son thereupon removed their
names from the circuit list.”

Mr. Berry Cass, writing to a Kyneton
(Vic.) paper, says “May I respectfully request
the favour of a few lines in reply to your extra-
ordinary report of my last night’s lecture. I am
perfectly astonished that you should have been
supplied with such a tissue of falsehoods. . . .
Instead of my having taken a negative view of
hell, I enforced, in the most positive terms, the
belief in a literal hell of fire and brimstone.” How
anxious this gentle Christian is to demonstrate
that he is not one of those soft-hearted persons

to Mr. Cass
A Port Darwin man stated in his census

The Australasian states that “a golden
thread has been drawn across the map of Austra-
lia by the Earl of Kintore,” and this simple but
first-class observatipn has gone down favourably
™ the nobility and aristocracy of the country.
And yet, when you come to think of it, it would
haveTioen a lot more to the point if it had stated
briefly that the Governor had dragged a red hair
across the continent. Kintore isn’t a spider who
leaves thread behind him in all his travels; and,
anyhow, he wasn’t walking on the map, so if he
had been a spider the thread wouldn’t have been
there. Moreover, he did nearly half the trip by
train, and for that part of the performance the
engine-driver had more to do with the thread
than he had. And, in any case, who in thunder
wants thread on a map? The thing is as much
out of place as wool on a lithograph orfluff on cold
meat. The statement isn’t fact, and it is inferior
metaphor, and dashed bad poetry. If the Aus-
tralasian had adhered strictly to the truth it
would have mentioned that footmarks, old boots,
meat-tins, and empty bottles had been deposited
in various parts of Australia by the red-haired
Ear] of Kin tore ye ken, together with his worn-
out hat and sundry disused shirts and socks ; and
the accuracy of the statement would have made up
for its want of halo and radiance. But the poor
old paper is always struggling to cast a glamour
round the clothes and victuals of the peerage, and
three times out of four it makes a dead failure
of it.
This is the Athenaeum,
And we are gathered here
To hold a Brewery Ballot,
In the name of the Muses—Beer!
Mr. Berry Cass,
Do you think it well
Thus to keep, alas,
Harping upon Hell?
Don’t you know it is—
If you don’t enquire—
Very stupid biz.
Thus to fool with fire?
What if it befell
That the Lord should make
A small private hell
For your special sake :
In which you should stew
While the ages pass—
Would that suit your view,
Mr. Berry Cass ?
Prithee be resigned,
Drop this subject drear !
Most of us can find
Hell enough up here.
Round the Shops and Shows.
One of the events of the past week has
been the commencement of Mrs. M‘Cathie’s
winter sale. As none but the very best goods are
sold at this establishment, no time should be lost
in securing some of the marvellous bargains.
Barge assortments of the most fashionable dress-
materials, mantles, millinery, &c., are marked at
tempting prices, whilst the gloves and hosiery are
especially good value. Most ladies know where
to find Mrs. M'Cathie’s, but for the few that don’t
it may be remarked that it is at 80 King-street.
* * *
An opal in the rough is not very pretty,
it has been in the hands of a competent
lapidary it is a thing of beauty and a joy for ever,
excepting to those who have foolish superstitions
about this most beautiful stone. Mr. Leon Harris,
of 41 Market-street, between Hunter’s Boot
Palace and Evening News office, deals exten-
sively in precious stones, and is ever ready to give
the best prices for pearls and onals in the rough.
Mr. Harris also makes a speciality of remodelling
old-fashioned jewellery.
* * #
Mr, Tollemache, of the City Buffet, is
fully rewarded for his kindly consideration to
the ladies in seeing the fair sex so liberally
patronise the room which opens off George-street.
They know how to appreciate a good dinner as
well as any one, and as it is here served in excel-
lent style by obliging waitresses it leaves nothing
to be desired. The ladies’ room, at the City
Buffet, needs only to be known to be popular.
# # *
Most people would like to know where
to obtain good tea, without adulteration, at a
reasonable price. There is a tea warehouse at No.
18 Royal Arcade, kept by Messrs. Harris & Co.,
where an excellent Ceylon tea is sold for 2s!
per pound. It only needs to be tested to be
appreciated.
* * #
Dr. P. Stanich has removed his consulting-
rooms to 20 Wynyard-square, where he can be
consulted daily. Dr. Stanich will devote his
attention to the deserving poor for ear, throat and
nose diseases every Tuesday.
*- * *
The problem of the day is how to keep
out the cold this wet, miserable weather. Nothing
better than a glass of Marie Brizard’s rum, which
is sold at all wine merchants, hotels and leading
grocers, in wicker bottles.

Mr. R. T. Vale in Reply.
Editor Bulletin.— Sir,— l have read your paper for many
years past, often with pleasure and profit, and I would
ask you for the favour of the insertion of a few lines in
reply to the article, “ Orange Pot v. Roman Kettle ”
You, as an old pressman, know how often newspapers
do not convey the language used, or fully express the
ideas expressed by a speaker. I must in this instance
refuse to accept the report you copy ; but I have no
desire to shirk the responsibility of any statements I make
on the public platform. I stated that the abbeys, mona-
steries and other ecclesiastical buildings in England were
destroyed by men who were born and brought up as
Roman Catholics; that in Catholic countries, such as
France, Italy and Spain, similar institutions were closed,
and the buildings destroyed or devoted to other purposes.
The statement as to the Cromwellian conflict is no
reply, for that was a fight on the one side by those who
upheld the divine right of kings, on the other by those
who thought the people had some rights. The reference
to prosecution of recusants in the time of Elizabeth simply
bears out my statement—they were carried out by those
who were born in the Catholic Church. The provocation
was that the recusants would not give their allegiance to
the State, but preferred to obey the Church, as in these
days. The aspect of the case that sent me into the
Orange Institution was the Dogma of Morals under which
the Catholic Church claims the right to decide for its
members how they should act and vote in all matters
they—the Church"—think fit. As one who has taken part
in the political life of Victoria for over 30 years, on what
I believe to be the Liberal side, I know that the Church
has exercised that power and its orders have beenobeyed.
I therefore learnt the bitter lesson that it was impossible
to work with men who were compelled to obey instruc-
tions given irrespective of the morals of the question at
issue.
In your reference to the wealth of the Church of Eng-
land, you omit to state that it is but a small remnant of
what was acquired by the Church of Rome at the expense
of the rest of the community. As to the other actions of
the Church of England, they claim no right to control the
action of its members in matters political.
I might go on further, but I have more regard for your
valuable space.
Yours, &a.
R. T. Valb
Ballarat, June 15, 1891.
[We print Mr. Vale's letter, though he makes no attempt
to answer our article.— Ed. B.]
All the first batch of N.S.W. parliamen-
tary candidates are hoarse de combat.
Wales is reported by an English paper to
have told his expostulating Ma that he gambled
because he wanted money. That is also the
reason, presumably, that he doesn’t pay when he
loses.
The Bulletin is amused to see that “ at a
dinner of the Victorian Employers’ Union on
Wednesday last week telegrams were read out
amid loud cheers announcing the victories of the
N.S.W, Government at the polls.” Moral, the
old one —“Never holler,” etc.

£lOO,OOO SWEEP.
(TO TUB EDITOR OF “ TUB AUSTRALIAN STAR.”)
Sir, —Will you, through your widely-circulated
paper, inform me and the general public if it is a
fact that “ Tattersall George Adams ” has disposed
of the whole or part of his interest in the well,
known Tattersall’s sweep, and that it is now
carried on by a syndicate of Sydney people ? If a
syndicate have it in hand will it be conducted in
the fair and square manner that it was supposed
to be when George Adams had it himself. —Yours,
&c„ A SUBSCRIBER.
(“The Australian Star,” Sydney, JuneS, 1891.)
“ TATTERSALL'S " CONSULTATIONS.
In answer to “A Subscriber,” whose letter we
published in our issue of yesterday, we have to
tate that upon inquiry we find there is no truth
whatever in the statement that “ Tattersall’s” coni
sultations have been disposed of to a syndicate-
The consultations are still conducted (and
will continue to be as heretofore) under
the direction and sole proprietorship of
“ Tattersall,” and the same principle of
drawing will always be adhered to.
