(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
PREFACE.
“ Stay me with flagons, comfort me with apples,
for lam sick of love.” Solomon, old man, you
had enough of it, if reports be true. Even I am
at present a little full-up of love, and have been
staying myself with flagons. Therefore, instead
of love, this time I choose Luck, Chance, Sors,
Fortuna, Kismet, or, in one word, “Life,” as my
theme. Lately, persons possessed of a white
swelling in the stom— — I mean waistcoat, and a
safe balance or credit in the banks, have been
trying to persuade us, the common people, that
“Industry leads to riches.” Now, where is the
Industry in holding a non-paying allotment where
gold is discovered ; or, of having a father who was
a thief ; or, of being descended from a who'esale
rum-seller; or, of marrying a hag with half-a-
milhon ; or, in fact, of being lucky? I am, there-
fore, about to tell you the story of a mate of mine
who goes by the name of Here’s Luck, and of my
mate’s mate who answers to Luck, and of the bad
luck that brought him (my mate), Luck, the good
luck. But first “ Stay mo with a flagon ! Here’s
Luck ! ”
Chapter I. — Luck.
Not many years ago a woman came, no one
knows how, on to a certain gold-field. She had a
bundle of clean, decent clothes, a clean, healthy
face, and a five-pound note. For the first night
she sought a bed at the Miners’ Home Hotel. For
the second and many more she stayed as cook and
laundress to the Warden’s wife —a lady who took
her without reference and allowed her pretty much
her own way. This washerwoman was reticent
bit civil, cheery but far from boisterous. I fancy
she is a Christian. (N.B.—Solomon was not.)
Ciiristian or heathen, however, she did a lot of
good. She washed the diggers’ moleskins at a
slulling a pair and often lent them the shilling
and gave them a cup of tea. Madame Warden
allowed her to do extra work, and liked to see the
diggers clean. There was, there is, one word that
this woman used in so many inflections that she
could express joy, sorrow, dislike, surprise, or
acquiescence with this monosyllable—Luck. And
at length she was called Luck, or Mrs. Luck. She
never said that that was not her name. Now,
there was a boozer who (let me use that splendid
Anglo-Saxon expression again) stayed himself
with flagons, until he could not swig his tea or
coffee, or a pint of water, without saying
Chapter ll.— Here’s Luck.
Thus, on these unnamed diggings there was
Luck and Here’s Luck. Jim (the latter’s name)
was dead-broke and owed four-and-six to Mrs.
Luck for washing, but he possessed a ticket in the
Dumbledee Freehold Art Union. With that he
paid his score, collared his rags and departed for
other diggings. Certainly he was advertised for
by Luck when she drew a £lOOO prize, but for
some years never a word did she hear of Jim,
The woman was no fool. With part of the thou-
sand she purchased a paying public-house and
dairy in, say, Manaro : and waited for Jim to put
in his claim for fifteen-and-six in the pound.
Again I ask, was it industry that made that pub.
and farm? Was it want of industry that caused
Jim to wander about among gullies and ridges,
when there was a woman ready to ante-up £775 to
him? Here’s Apropos to nothing. I’ll chip in
with a story of myself.
Chapter ITT.— Bad Luck,
I was draftsman to a tramway-company and
had a holiday because it was New Year’s Day, a
day sacred to the god Here’s!ye in Scotland. My
chum, Willie Murray, sharer of our common
sitting-room, asked me to give him help in finish-
ing some tracings that were badly wanted on the
next day, January the 2nd. Luckily he asked
me at breakfast, and his office was near at hand.
The last words I printed were Bald and Wild ,
Accountants, Agents —right in the corner. Now,
Air. Bald was my agent, and had a few pounds of
mine in his hand. Therefore I took the trouble to
look over the original of the tracing. It was a
new opening of an old mine in Spain—call it the
Tradio Copper-mining Company, Limited. Take
notice that Bald and Wild are the smartest
chartered accountants in Glasgow, and there-
fore in the world. Next morning I
nobbled Mr. Bald on the stair and in-
sisted on his investing all the cash he held of mine
in deposit money at 10 per cent, in Tarpios.
“ Whab the deil do ye ken aboot Tarpio ? ”
That’s what he said. But I let him know that it
had come to my understanding how he was float-
ing a big company and that I must have 40 or 50
f reference shares. “ All taken up, Air. Wrinkler.”
then suggested that no elders of the Free Church
dare do that before the lists were opened on the
Exohange. “All right, my boy: in memory of
the respect in which I held your deceased grand-
mother, I’ll see you get them.” And I did.
Now, white paunches, was it want of industry
that lost all of us a chance, just because Spain
indulged in one of its periodical revolutions ? It
was Luck, Bad Luck, Dombad Luck, In this
way, instead of driving chariots in Park Terrace,
Glasgow, I came to Manaro Diggings and at Mrs.
Luck’s sign of the Golden Hall, I was—same
again, when in walked a very muddy man, not in
the best of tempers, either. But the landlady
said, “ Luck ! Here’s Luck.”
Chapter IV.— Mixed Luck.
I must go back a month with Jim. He had
tried every gully for alluvial and every spur for a
reef, and was very stiff. A publican asked him
t® raise a few tons of stone from a trial shaft,
promising £6 for the job. Then he came out to
him and offered a cheque. Perhaps the bit of
paper was right enough, but Jim wanted cash,
and went to the publican next day and received
six notes. One, “Old Bob,” a shepherd, was
there suffering a recovery, and offered to show a
reef for two drinks. He got the drinks and was
told by Jim that he had been had too often, and
did not want to be laid on. Then Jim said,
“ Here’s Luck ” until he had no more coin, and
went away sorrowful unto his camp.
On his way home he came across a man, patch-
ing his only trousers, and therefore bare-legged.
This poor fellow had been backed up by his
brother for more than a year, looking
for gold, but had not seen a speck.
Then the assistance was stopped, and he
was just mending his pants for his home-
ward journey. Close to the start he met “Old
Bob,” and for two drinks was shown the reef,
which Jim refused, and on which he and the
publican were glad to peg off No. 1 North, This
reef, the General Jackson (prospector was a
Yankee from Yackandandah), paid £5OOO in divi-
dends for the prospectors and not a speck in No. 1
North ! Where does the industry come in here ?
In the mistrust of the cheque, in the flagons, in
the two drinks, or in the patching of the bare-
legged Yankee’s pants ?
Chapter V. and Last.—Good Luck.
Jim was offered his divvy and refused it because
he had reckoned on giving away the whole lottery-
chance for four-and-six. Then Jim offered him-
self and as Luck could see no other way of pay-
ment the firm is now Luck and Here’s Luck or
Mr. and Mrs. Jim, and the sign is the “ Lucky
Digger.”
SCOTTY THE WEINKLER.
A WONDERFUL DANE.
Herr Rassmussen, the celebrated Danish Her-
balist and proprietor of the famous Denmark
pills, has only just completed his 30th year of
age, and although he is one of the most successful
of Australian medical men, he is one of the most
modest and unselfish sort of beings we have ever
come across. His patent medicines have become
famous and are used almost all through the world
and bid fair to become the most popular of all
known remedies. Herr Rassmussen holds over 50
patents and registrations for his remedies, and
although he is in possession of a Registration
diploma from the Government of N.S. Wales,
as well as from Queensland, entitling him to call
his remedies “Dr. Rassmussen’s remedies,” he
nevertheless calls them plain “Herr Rass-
mussen’s,” which designation he thinks is quite
sufficient to guarantee their virtues and command
the confidence of the public, as he sells more pills
in Australia than any other medicine-man. He
claims that his remedies have cured over 50,000
sufferers, and his numerous testimonials go far to
prove it. He was also presented with a magnifi-
cent gold medal by several of his cured patients a
short time ago, and he is a credit to his profession.
He has written several good medical works, the
principal one of which is “ The Natural Doctor,”
a valuable addition to the medical literature of
this colony.*
".The recent Anglo-Manipuri “fight” is
described as a “ heavy combat.” Now the British
who atacked the enemy’s trenches had but twelve
(chiefly niggers) wounded and two (niggers) killed,
while the Manipuris left behind them 128 actually
slain besides many slaughtered by the pursuing
cavalry. Battue, not battle, is the word.
When Premier Munro was in Scotland,
more than a year ago, he addressed a temperance-
meeting, which he favoured with a little historical
account of Australia. Of old-time bushrangers,
he remarked that some were shot and others
hanged. Of publicans, he told how Sunday-
trad ers were fined £5, £lO and £l5 respectively for
first, second and third offences. The Scotch
reporter who was present got his temperance
“ notes ” mixed, and the local press came out
with a report which made Munro assert that
second-offence publicans were “shot or hanged,
according to the degree of their crimes.” Soon
afterwards Munro received a letter from a Scottish
Women’s Temperance Union, asking for fuller
particulars respecting Australian licensing laws
and their penalties. When the S.W.T.XJ.
learned the true and tame condition of things, it
sent Munro a formal acknowledgment, thanking
him for his courtesy, but regretting that the
published report which had misled them teas nut

WHAT EXPERIENCE TEACHES
IS BEST.
There is something to consider in the daily ex-
penses for the household necessities, which in the
aggregate of the year amount to a great deal.
The ethics of buying and selling appear to be for
the vendor to get all he can, and for the purchaser
to see that he don’t get too .much. But what is
too much ? It is almost a profound question, and
presents the strange paradox that the dearest in
many ways is often the cheapest. Certainly thi s
is so in food, when health is and in
remedies which restore health after it has been
impaired. It is certainly so in clothing, for a
cheap suit that will hardly last one season is
dearer than the one that will last two seasons, the
difference in price being reasonable. So in shoes
and the like. Recurring to the items of food and
health, undoubtedly the most important, it is
found that villainous adulteration is what renders
it cheaper in much that is sold, and men are
known who have spent thousands to be cured of
disease, have' suffered years of agony, and have
trifled away their substance on worthless remedies.
That which is testified to by thousands as being
an absolute cure, and permanently efficacious, is
cheap at any price in comparison with such as
have no virtue, and which prolong suffering. A
case in point is the following: “New Bloom-
field, Pa., April 26, 1886. The Charles A.
Vogeler Co., Baltimore, Md. Gentlemen. —For
more than 30 years I had been afflicted with rheu-
matism so severe that I had to use morphine to
secure rest at night. Spent hundreds of dollars
with physicians and for remedies without benefit.
Five years ago I tried St. Jacobs oil, and it
effected an entire and permanent cure. Cold or
damp weather does not affect me at all. I desire
to give my unqualified endorsement. —J. E. Bon-
sall, clerk of the several Courts of Perry County
Pa.” The point here is not so much what Mr.
Bonsall paid for the great remedy for pain, for
the price is a mere bagatelle, but that he was per-
manently cured after 30 years’ suffering. Of
course the poor must count the cost in everything;
but they should reckon on the sound basis that
that which is bad is worthless, and that which is
the best and will cure, and stay cured, is cheap at
any price.
THE LADIES’ WATERBURY WATCH.
Is a plain nickel-plated time-keeper, which per-
forms its functions as well in water as it does in
the owner’s pocket, and is sold for 22s Cd by all
store-keepers and dealers throughout the colonies.
Persons afflicted with doubt as to the advertised
merits of the wonderful Waterbury Watches can
inspect the originals of all published testimonials
on application at the company’s head Austral-
asian offices in Sydney, or they can buy a Water-
bury, and test it in competition with one of the
“cheap English levers” constructed for the
benefit of the credulous, composed of nine-tenths of
case, and the remainder stamped rubbishy Swiss
mechanism; which accounts for all the milk there
is in that particular cocoanut. The Ladies’
Waterbury (Series “ L ”), like the other products
of this world-famed factory, is only just what is
claimed for it—a perfectly reliable timekeeper,
equally adapted to the necessities of a live duchess
or a washerwoman, and sold at a price that places
it within the reach of all classes. The watch
itself has since its introduction given universal
satisfaction. It is not only an accurate, but also
a neat and elegant little time-keeper, similar in
construction to the new short-winding gentlemen’s
Waterbury (Series “J”), but smaller, and with-
out seconds-hand. Mr. Chas. J. Horstey, J.P.,
O.P.S , Mudgee, N.S.W., who is the happy pos-
sessor of one of these watches, writes : “ I have
much pleasure in stating that my Waterbury
Watch (a Series “ L ”) is without exception one of
the best watches I ever owned, although I have
had many more expensive. It is a reliable and
excellent time-keeper.” To be obtained from any
respectable store keeper or dealer ; price, 22s 6d.

Various Verses.
After Dinner.
Framed in our old verandah-chair
(The sea-side air and sunset braving),
She seems a picture, still and fair,
Her fan of feathers scarcely waving:
Dressed all in crimson, from the slip
Of airy gauze that crowns her tresses,
To satin shoe’s embroidered tip ;
(Her stockings always match her dresses).
So sweet she looks, one halt believes
She must be some Venetian lady
Come back to life (with hanging sleeves)
From marble palace, grim and shady :
Some people think she is a dunce,
And some find fault with her complexion j
You do not see these faults at once,
But only after long reflection.
And near her bends the man of Law-
Heavy his brow with mystic learning—
His fingers trifle with a straw ;
His eyes are dark, and sad, and burning ;
Perhaps he speaks a tender word
Or fragment of some old love-ballad,
But this is all 1 overheard—
“ The proper way to make a salad .”
Annie Glknny Wilson (of N.Z).
—The Century.
Family Financing.
‘‘ They tell me you work for four shillings a clay ;
How is it you clothe your six boys on such pay ?”
“ I know you will think me conceited and queer,
But I do it because I’m a good financier.
“ There’s Pete, John, Jim, Joe, William and Ned,
A half-dozen boys to be clothed up and fed ;
“ And I buy for them all good, plain victuals to eat;
But clothing—l only buy clothing for Pete.
“ When Pete’s clothes are too small for him to get on
My wife makes ’em over and gives ’em to John.
“ When for John, who is ten, they have grown out of
date,
She just makes ’em over to Jim, who is eight.
“ When for Jim they’ve become too ragged to fix,
She just makes ’em over for Joe, who is six.
“ And when little Joseph can wear ’em no more,
She just makes ’em over for Bill, who is four.
“ And when for young Bill they no longer will do,
She just makes ’em over for Ned, who is two.
“ So you see, if I get enough clothing for Pete,
The family is furnished with clothing complete.”
“ But when Ned has got through with the clothing, and
when
He has thrown it aside—what do you with it then ?”

“The Pivot.”
Within that dark and silent town,
No living being stirred ;
In vain I wandered up and down
No sound of life I heard.
The gloomy silence of the scene
Weighed on my soul like lead ;
So strangely still, it might have been
A city of the dead.
I called on Heav’n—nor called in vain,
A vision blest my sight;
For, gleaming through a window-pane,
I saw a candle-light.
I reached the door—my eager knocks
Called forth an ancient dame ;
With wrinkled skin and scanty locks,
And age down-bedoubled frame.
“ What town is this? lone dame,” I said,
“ Of light and life bereft—
Its people all, I see, are dead,
And you alone are left.”
“ Young man, you’re altogether wrong,”
My aged informant said—
“ This noble city is Geelong;
It’s people are in bed !”
Australia; A Patriotic Poem.
Fair land, replete in all good things,
Great men, and hardwood logs,
Materials for Irish stew,
And lots of blushing poets, too,
And aldermen, and hogs.
Fair mistress of the Southern seas,
Land of the fiercely fizzing flood
Of beer and tea; oh, land of rocks,
(All taken up as mining-blocks),
Of dust, likewise of mud.
Oh, land of mountains—and of flats ;
Whose stalwart sons can hold their own ;
Oh, land of love, tight boots and bunions,
Bright eyes, red lips, and steak-and-onions ;
Oh, land that well can go a loan.
Oh, land (we do, each acre’s mortgaged)
No blood pollutes thy wide Domain,
Except blue-blooded earls and dukes
Who camp within its sheltered nooks
And sigh for “ home ” in vain.
Oh, land (past rhymers have subsisted
On that one phrase, so now I’ll kill it),
Oh, land where every citizen
Before he’s reached the age of ten
Expects a Guv’ment billet.
Oh, land, whose eggshell cradle floats
In latitudes elysian—
But why should I sing higher than
That heavenly inspired man—
The average politician.
Redfern,
E.J.D.
The latest thing in cosmetics is a kind of
scarlet grease, which is sold in a pot and used to
paint the gums. It is nasty to taste, and the
grease “ runs,” so that the beauteous female who
uses it feels as if she had an old candle-end in her
mouth, and it isn’t over-nice for the outside party
who kisses her, for he finds little bits of the sub-
stance on her lips ; but then it lends a new dazzle
to her smile, and when she opens her mouth it
makes her teeth look like a row of snowballs in a
butcher’s shop. Still it looks to' the unbiased
critic as if this paint business was getting to be a
trifle overdone. There is very little about a really
high-toned female except her feet and her wind-
pipe that isn’t painted nowadays.

An Auckland (N.Z.) citizen still possesses
—or is said to possess—the old imperial crown of
King Cakobau of Fiji. Cakobau struggled through
most of his reign without a crown, but, becoming
civilised, he called for tenders in his old age for
the manufacture of that emblem of sovereignty.
The successful tenderer—a local blacksmith —
undertook to do the whole business, labour and
material included, for ten shillings; and he fixed
up a really good crown out of a sawed-off meat-tin
covered with velvet. But Cakobau, in true
monarchical style, refused to pay, and when he
was out in regal state, with the meat-tin tiara on
his head, and looking every inch a king, the black-
smith used to run behind him howling for his
half-sovereign, and so he ran and howled till the
last day of Cakohau’s reign. He is still unpaid,
and his descendants continue to vociferate in vain
for the money just as the heirs of Wales’ tailor
will vociferate in the middle of next century and
got nothing for their pains.
Melbourne Post-office clock was persis-
tently and discordantly “loyal” on Queen’s
Birthday. It chimed “God Save,” &c., and
“ Rule Britannia,” one down, t’other come up, for
about sixteen hours, more or less, and the imme-
diate neighbourhood had become a hot-bed of Re-
publicanism long before midnight. These
mechanical contrivances for strengthening the
Throne are always apt to work in a wrong direc-
tion. The Muse will explain :
BROWN'S COVERSION.
Old Brown he was a Jingo true,
With appetite voracious
For all the yarns relating to
The goodness of Her Gracious;
He praised the Queen with heart and voice,
Until it used to bore us
To hear the worthy snob rejoice
That Y. was reigning o’er us.
Of all the folks in Melbourne town
Whose forms are fat and heavy,
Not one could beat Elijah Brown
At rushing to a levee.
He sang, in no uncertain tone,
The Anthem—nay, he bawled it;
That England gave him birth ’twas known —
His “mother-land ” he called it.
For thirty years this person stout
Loved institutions regal;
The Bulletin he’d simply scout
As something most illegal;
In politics our hero ranged
Up close to Mrs. Grundy,
But all his “ views ” and notions changed
At once, the other Monday.
Old Brown was blowing round the “Block”
(A ramble to his liking),
He heard the new Post-otficc clock
Its loyal ditties striking :
Whilst others covered up their ears
And thought the racket frightful,
He said, twixt apoplectic cheers,
“ Now isn’t that delightful! ”
But when again, and yet again
The clock had chimed “ God Save ” her,
The joy of Brown began to wane,
The tune had lost its savour;
He felt his ardour slowly cool,
His brain begin to soften—
He wished Britannia wouldn’t rule
The Waves so doosid often.
“ It must be so,” he murmured low,
t “The truth there’s no gainsaying—
This awful clock was made to show
The game that Vve been playing ;
I, too, have been a mere machine
(Until I’ve nearly busted)
For striking up ‘God Save the Queen,*
With which I’m now disgusted.”
Brown crushed his hat upon his head,
And left off looking pompous ;
Without another word he fled
From that infernal rumpus.
A boy was shouting “ Argus ! ” and
More journals of that kidney ;
But Brown, with sixpence in his hand,
Said “ Bulletin—the Sydney.”

846 Maequarie-street North, Sydney, June 24,1891,
Mr Dear Moorabinua,—
Popular as the Jerseys already are, there are a lot of
people who view their gyrations with the fishy eye of dis-
affection, and those are the people who during the
Caringtonian era used to invite themselves to the vice-
regal marble halls, but who now never get any further
than the sentry-box. Lady Jersey objects, it would
appear, to make herself the target for people who are far
from the Pure Merino social fold, or to admit into her
drawing-room drapery-delegates sent by their firms for
fashion-tips. In the sacred vice-regal precincts one no
longer shoulders barmaids, housemaids and cooks (at
least not in an active sense), because Lady Jersey invites
her persecutors. Nevertheless the vice-regal invitations
are strewn tolerably broadcast, and the clique that so
successfully “ran” little Lady C. has been broken up
and scattered to the four winds of Heaven. And thus it
is that there are people who chafe at the Jersey idea of
no public receptions and who mourn for the free-and-
easy Caringtons in whom, so far as the manner of their
official receptions was concerned, democracy was in-
carnate.
The second Jersey June “ at home” happened on 16th
inst., when the familiar elements of girls, men, flowers,
waxy floor and good music, mixed together, resulted, as
usual, in a happy little dance. Everybody arrived shortly
before and after 9, and when, at a quarter past. Lord and
Lady Jersey entered the drawing-room, the guest-list was
almost complete. Then the first square struck up and
women began to wonder what their neighbours had got on*
Lady Jersey, whom everybody gushes over as a hostess >
wore pale-blue brocade and diamonds; her daughters, in
cream striped silk, finished off with a little lace ; a some-
what Bernhardtish garment worn by a belle just back
from Hingland, and who talks like it, was in tiger-yellow
silk, draped in some strange way, and relieved by a big
loose bunch of violets ; a titled woman looked historical
in pink silk and diamonds, and there were one or tw
debutantes who became wreathed in ecstatic smiles at thy
approach of every partner. A “ decidedly gyp ” suppe r
(as Don Alameda would say) put a lustrous top-polish on
this pleasing occasion.
The first vice-regal visit to Sydney Theatre Royal
happened on 22nd inst., when a full and frocky house
and the vice-regal box draped in Jersey colours proclaimed
a special occasion. Lady Jersey wore white silk, com-
pletely covered with black lace, dark-grey gloves, and
diamond necklace and bangles, and was mainly remark-
able for studiously studying her programme whenever
the “stage-love” got up to squeezing-point. Her three
daughters wore pink frocks and bows in their hair, and
mamma and the girls all carried handsome managerial
flowers. Despite the frogs’ weather outside, the circle
was full dress. In the much-fought-over O.P. box, the
lady litigant appeared in heliotrope chiffon and diamonds.
another nice frock was of strawberry gauz'e, embroidered
in crimson filoselle, worn with a cream and satin opera-
cloak ; and a damsel of hour-glass outline was a refreshing
sight in spring-green silk and a cluster of oak-leaves in
her classical (op-knot. But one weird, Potterish belle,
got-up in black, garlanded k la prize ox with pink roses’,
unwisely left a dilapidated and muddy hoof-case
trailing out into the aisle from under her flowing robes.
“Gondoliers” went splendidly, only why does sweet-
voiced Miss Young paint her face bright pink and leave
her neck and ears pipe-clay white ? It’s clownish.
The ball in aid of the Sydney Nurses’ Home was not an
astonishing numerical success, perhaps because the even-
ing was wretchedly cheerless and sloppy, and people
dreaded beating up the Town Hall steps (which are never
sheltered) from their vehicles. Still there were enough
present to keep things going, and as furniture, decora-
tions, flowers, all necessary wines, programmes, and bill-
posters were given gratuitously by several Sydney firms,
the Home is sure to benefit largely by the ball. The
Nurses' ball will probably plume itself over the fact of its
being the first public ball patronised by Lord and Lady
Jersey, who were both there, Lady Jersey wearing a
trained gown of carubia-red brocade lit up by magnificent
diamonds. Rather a fancy-dress effect was lent to the
floor by the presence of the nurses in their lilac-print
gowns and white caps and aprons. Many of the sisters
Dora objected to the order that uniform should be worn
instead of full-dress, and certainly the print skirts looked
rather skimpy beside the billows of tulle and chiffon.
There Are one or two rags that one did more than wink
at. was of shell-pink brocade, the pattern out-
lined in silver tinsel, which formed the bodice and train,
the plain front of silver-grey merv edged at the feet with
a pink ruche of pink silk ; folds of grey and pink chiffon
finished off the bodice, while diamonds lent their own par-
ticular charm'to hair and corsage. The wearer was a tall
fair woman who carried off her clothing, as we say in
France. Another poetic outer garment had the bodice
and fan-tail train of striped lavender and white brocade,
the jupe of white Chantilly lace looped here and there
with lilac, which appeared on the shoulder and fell in
trails from the right hip; another garment one couldn’t
exactly quarrel with was in eiel-blue bengaline made very
plain with a long, thin Potter train, the bodice softened
with nicely-drawn blue chiffon studded here and there
with forget-me-nots, a “jewelled” girdle in sapphires
and turquoises finishing off the gown. All the leading
Sydney Sawboneses were present, with their wives and
One of the most interesting sights at the Sydney
Nurses’ ball, the other night, was the spectacle of a very
vary exalted personage standing meekly on one leg and
awkwardly nursing the gigantic bouquet which his
radiant partner thrust coquettishly into his arms when
she sat down to supper. If the flowers had Wen of a good
glaring crimson they might have of the
cardinal out of his love-locks, hut as it wast— '
Speaking of the Nurses’ ball, what might be called the
“ bathing-house effect,” was greatly en evidence at this
deserving festival—that is, bare-necked women with their
wraps hanging like towels round their shoulders. What
possible warmth there'is in only passing your cloak over
your back and collar-bones while you leave your snowy
buzzum still a prey to corner draughts is one of the
mysteries of feminine “ wrapping up.” But what woman
of them all dreams of fastening a Wadded cloak over her
breast-bone when the heating dance is o’er !
A Sydney Women’s Union (not to be too explicit) had a
“ nasty jar ” from Government House, the other day.
Their secretary wrote a long ranting epistle to Lady J.,
setting forth the confidence they had in her firm deter-
mination to see her sex “righted” and woman made to
wave in the front of every movement, &c., &c. But the
ardour of the,Union was considerably damped when they
received a cold answer from Lady J. expressing her
disapproval of their proceedings, and refusing to allow
them to use her name in any way. And yet there are
some people who say that Lady Jersey is the mainspring
of the present frantic female movement!
One couldn’t help turning one’s thumbs at “Adrienne
Leoouvreur,” at the SydneyQarrick on 20th inst.,although
Janet Achurch in the name-part worked like a horse in a
Saxon wig, and Charrington was about at his best as
prompter “ Mishonay.” But a deadly air of unreality and
long and awful waits wearied out the d.c., whose people
were too few to keep the house warm, and Maggie Moore
in black silk and lace and a hunch of roses and jonquils
was the only woman in evening dress.. On the stage the
frocking was dismal. As the poisoning princess, Bronton
wore two modern tea-gowns, though the date was 1730
Jerky Alice May, as the duchess (why does she pronounce
to “ toe?”), fitfully went and came in a garment reminis-
cent of a bottle of ink upset in a butcher’s shop, and even
Miss Achurch, who generally ’tires herself prettily, was
patchy. Her only real dress was the soft cream gown of
the death-scene, which went very well. By the way, I
want to point out to Adrienne that it isn’t customary for
ladies to gently clutch their lover's hand in erotic move-
ments. A vulgar young man once defined love to me as
“an itch in your heart and you can’t scratch it.” Is this
why Adrienne gently scraped-the warlike paw of Maurioe?
The first Hutcheson concert at Y.M.C.A Hall, held
on a soaking wet and sloppy evening, only drew a handful
of surprised-looking people in dingy clothes, altho’ Marian
Burton —attended by George Leitch and hubby Waterlow
—wore a handsome cloak of maize satin designed in gold
filigree, and edged with white feather fur, in which she
looked charming. Ernest played four “ pieces ” and was
encored for each, and certainly he deserved the compli-
ment. He is only a boy and performs like one, so far as
thrill and weird soulful shudder are concerned, but then
he plays conscientiously, with an almost pious regard for
all the steering-marks, set up by the composer, and the
result pleases you even if you are not exactly electrified.
Ernest may put his palpitating soul into it by and by when
some false-haired one has blighted his existence for him,
but I do not think the sympathetic will be his style ;
when matured, it probably will be mechanically brilliant.
The pianist was assisted by Colbourne-Baber in white
brocade and pink roses, Master Leader, and a fat and bald
male nightingale with a lilt like the cry of a herd of
cattle, who seemed to lift his voice up with both hands
and hurl it at the audience. Of course he sang “ For
Ever and for Ever.”
I have received Madame VVeigal’s fashion journal for
June, and regret to say that the gifted diagramist hasn’t
one positive novelty for a woman to clutch at. To be told
that the newest thing in bodices is the Louis XV. style,
when you have one, half worn-out, hanging up in your
frock-press, does not make you regard the journal as an
infallible sign-post to the extremist, and most of the hats
advocated are enough to make a black gin weep. Some
of the crochet patterns, however, may be handy for the
house fairy to whom winter and wool-work are synonyms.
From Melbourne I have received a cold and malignant
epistle mincing up the Austral Salon welcome to
’Opetoun’s little mavourneen. After fifteen pages of
scathing personal allusions to the legs and arms of the
lady artists, I was glad to see a word of praise for the
gentleman who essayed “King Arthur but the women
get nothing save abuse. Yet from another source lam
told that the show was a huge success. Which was it?
It is so very hard to get facts out of any woman.
Apropos of the vice-regal visit to the Royal, I couldn’t
help noticing that Lady Jersey sat on the right-hand side
of the V.R. box, where Carington always sat in all his
yawning glory; while Lord Jersey (he looks meeker than
ever in evening dress) occupied the left-hand chair then
occupied by Lady Carington.
Yours affectionately,
SAppiio Smith.
Tempe Refuge ball is to happen in Sydney Centennial
Hall on 29th July. Tickets are already being sold, and
Lord and Lady Jersey have promised both patronage and
presence.
The N.S.W. Officers’ Amateur theatricals in the New
Gymnasium, Victoria Barracks, on 17th inst., went off
very smoothly—one society lady pervading the stage in
quite a professional manner. Byron's “ Partner for Life ”
was the piece, and the voice of the prompter wasn’t as
audible as it usually is on such occasions.
The latest maritime wedding was that of Captain T. R.
Thompson, assistant harbour master of Sydney, to. Miss
Louie Hughes, daughter of Alderman Harvey Hughes, of
Petersham, on 17th inst. Loads of congratulatory gifts,
including one from the City Bowling Club, of which the
bridegroom is a member.
The parents of young “Tom” Curran, who leaves for
England by the Oroya with the object of pursuing his
studies at Oxford, entertained a large party of friends at
a down-the-harbour picnic on Saturday last. The young
student, a fine specimen of our Australian youth, took
high honours at St. Ignatius’ College (where he edited the
journal), in the class-room and on the river, and is certain
to do the land of his nativity great credit.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The best on record was when Nick Judge ran Davy
Snag for £2O and the mile championship of the district.
When the challenge was issued by Nick the boys tumbled
over each other in their eagerness to put up the money.
Nick was a horse-breaker, and it was well-known that he
couldn’t run for sour apples, while Snag could break 4.40
without training. However, after the first excitement of
making the match the boys began to recollect numerous
occasions on which Nick had proved himself a particularly
downy bird, and an uneasy suspicion forced itself upon
them that things were not what they seemed, and that
Nick would at the last moment produce the right bower.
They sat up nearly all night discussing the possible and
impossible tricks which Nick might resort to, till at last
Rixon said: “1 am sure there must be a horse in it
somewhere,” whereupon Brown burst into laughter and
shouted, “Yes, and we’ll euchre him at his own game.”
At the appointed time the men faced the starter, the pistol
was fired, and away went the runners, Davy bounding
along with the light, easy stride of the professional miler,
and Nick following, with the straddling gait of
the habitual horseman. Before they had gone a
hundred yards, however, Nick’s stable-boy, mounted on
a long-tailed pony, rode out of the hush and cantered in
front of Nick, who seized the pony’s tail and roared out
to the boy to go ahead. The pony, with Nick in tow,
rapidly overhauled Davy and passed him, when suddenly
Davy’s brother, mounted on a racehorse, made his
appearance from behind a haystack and, galloping up to
Davy, called out to him to catch hold. Now the fun
began in real earnest. Nick and the pony had a long
start, but blood and condition soon began to tell. The
boys yelled and danced and shouted themselves hoarse
as Davy, hanging to the race-horse's tail, came leaping
along with giant strides and.catching Nick at the half-mile
post, passed him, and sailed home like an electrified
kangaroo. Yon never heard such laughing as there was
in Blewitt’s hotel when the stakes were paid over. And
Nick was so unmercifully chaffed about the mile-champion-
ship that he left the district shortly afterwards. Davy’s
time, taken by several reliable stop-watches, was 3.124
just about a minute better than the previous best on
record.

A growing evil of these times is the publication
of Slavin’s portrait in weekly and evening newspapers.
Some years have elapsed since the outline of that genial
(we call him genial because he can hit so hard) Irishman’s
face sent the first thrills through this continent, so by
degrees even amateur athletes have become hardened to
the spectacle, and find its constant repetition a weariness
to the spirit. The excuse for allowing Slavin to haunt
journals of the “ enterprising ” kind are weak and various.
He is generally set up, together with a descriptive
account of his age and fighting weight, directly some
mysterious “ preliminaries” have been arranged, or nego-
tiations entered into, by the broken-nosed nuisances who
are reported to be . “at his back.” Then the details are
finally settled to the satisfaction of all parties, and Slavin
is handed to the printer again. Presently he appears as
the Hero of the recent great Glove Contest, soon after
which they have got round to preliminaries once more,
followed by settled details and another victory, with a
portrait on each occasion. What makes this intermittent
calamity more aggravating is the obstinacy with which
they stick to the same old genuine view of Slavin, instead of
inserting Battenberg, or the Narbethong murderer, or
some such established attraction by way of a change. A
pugilist’s face is naturally as monotonous as six pounds of,
cold corned beef in a household comprising two souls, and
a stereotyped picture of it is more tiring than an epi-
demic. The Bulletin desires that Slavin may be with-
drawn from circulation for awhile, unless an imaginative
artist is prepared to anticipate the future by sketching
him as a prosperous pub-keeper, in about his fortieth
year. Or they might give the world a bewildering idea of
Miss Slavin, a large, ruminative young lady who towers
over the up-stairs bar of a Melbourne hotel. She deserves
to be brought into notice, not only for the sake of her fine
physique, but also on account of her commercial insight
into things and absolute freedom from hysterics. If you
say to her—“ Miss Slavin, your brother is the greatest
fighter this side of Heaven,” she will reply—“ Well, I
hope he’s making as much money at it as they say—” and
then smile dubiously at the surrounding bottles.

One of our smartest owners made a bit of a
mistake, the other day, in commissioning an up-eountry
friend to lay out a hundred pounds for him. Our friend,
the owner, had a horse—we’ll say Yarraman—running in
a race at Randwick, and picked upon the countryman to
back him, thinking to do better if the wager were taken
by a man not known to the Israelites. “ Two to one
against the field,” the betting men were yelling, when up
goes “mug” to one of them and says, “Book me two
hundred to a hundred about Yarraman.” Yarraman, at
the time, was at 7 to 1, and the language of the owner
may be better imagined than described when his horse
won and he learned how the yokel had laid his money out

The Australian daily newspaper is always at its
stupidest when dealing with racing matters “ at home.”
Melbourne Herald, for example, mentions that Mons Meg
followed up her success in the Gold Vase by running
fourth in the Ascot Gold Oup, as though the latter per-
formance were something to blow about. Knowing the
average strength of fields for the Ascot Cup The Bulletin
imagines that Mons Meg finished last.
* # #
Lord Sheffield’s influence on the forthcoming
English Eleven will be beneficial, we imagine. He seems
genuinely fond of cricketers for cricket’s sake, and, as the
“ noble patron ” of the entire company, he will not
encourage the players who put “Mr.” in front of their
names to slight the pros. Sheffield should serve to take
the rough edge off the social distinction difficulty, if he
be a man of good tact, while, in a sporting sense, he is
certain to keep the ball rolling. The professionals can do
wonderful things when there’s a wealthy ludship ready to
pay, if necessary, a £5 note for every wicket they take.

Horse-story from a recent London Life
“ During a very severe frost a few years ago, Mr. John
Milburn, farm-manager to the Durham County Asylum
(whose death has occurred this week), was saved from a
most dangerous predicament by the sagacity of his horse.
He was riding home one night, and when near his own
door the animal slipped and Mr. Milburn was thrown '
heavily and lay stunned. So keen was the frost that his
clothes practically froze to the ground, and he would
inevitably have perished, but that the faithful horse, with
its warm tongue, licked the face and head of its master
until a rescue-party arrived.” Yah !

Fame. Last week Melbourne Standard pub-
lished a small portrait with the following biographical
legend ; —“ E. Warren, who for the first time was elected
this season as captain, has been a constant player in the
Williamstown club for the past eight years, and under the
seasiders’ colours has gajned distinction as a goal-sneak.”
Fancy gaining distinction as a goal-sneak ! How should
a goal-sneak’s epitaph be written ? Let Shakespeare, who
has said something fine about everything, suggest:—
The Y.R.C. threatened to prevent the reception
of fictitious advertising entries for this year’s Melbourne
Cup, but it would seem that other counsels have pre-
vailed. Come to think of it, this means of advertising is
remarkably cheap. Every paper in Australasia publishes
the list of nominations for the Cup, and the cute entrant
of a patent medicine “prad” obtains several million
printings of its name for his fiver. The Bulletin predicts
that in a year or two the idea will be elaborated further,
and we will have a horse named somebody’s soap, or
cocoa, or electro-medical soles, winning the world’s
biggest handicap.
* # #
The “ Sowth ” Melbourne football team, which
at present seems a “dead bird ” for the Victorian premier-
ship, has only two men in the crowd who have not
played for some other club. Their last year’s balance-
sheet shows an expenditure of £2soo—chiefly on cab-hire,
postage and incidental expenses, apparently.
# # #
It is related that millionaire “ Abington ” Baird,
familiarly known as “ the Squire,” was lately at a Sunday
evening “small and early,” entertaining, among others,
the secretary of a big sporting club and a “Society actor,”
who is considerably “gone on himself” as a boxer. The
talk turning somewhat naturally upon punching, the
actor made very strong running', enumerating in rapid
succession dozens of doughty knights of the mitten he
had knocked out. - “ But did you ever come across Piggy
Bettinson ?” asked the secretary. “ Did I? 1 rather
think I did—once—ask him about it,” responded the
buskin, ambiguously, but with an assuring smile. “ Well,
I’ve no occasion to,” chimed in the other, “for he was
telling me about it only last night. He said you asked
him up to your place, and that you’d got all your kids,
with their best bibs and tuckers on, sitting up on the side-
board to see you lay him out. The first round didn’t last
a minute (and it wasn’t Piggy who was ‘ out ’), when your
eldest kid slipped off his perch, and, running to the head
of the stairs, called—‘Ma, come up ; I’se afraid poor pa’s
dead!’”
* * ■*
How are the mighty fallen ! In the recent
Jackson-Corbett fight, at the California Athletic Club,
Frisco, Peter wore a porous plaster on his stomach. If
this sort of thing continues subsequent developments
will show the cheerful slugger coming out to mortal
combat with Somebody’s Pills pasted across his manly
bosom.
* * #
The following anent J. L. Sullivan and Peter
Jackson appears in San Francisco Daily Report of 28th
May;—“An affair which came near a bare-knuckle fight
took place in Jackson’s saloon on Turk-street last night.
Sullivan and Jackson met for the first time, and they were
angry and their eyes flashed as they recognised one
another. Both were on the verge of a hard round or two
when friends interfered and separated them. At mid-
night Sullivan paid a visit to Jackson's saloon with a few
of his (Sullivan's) friends. The story, told by one who
saw the trouble and listened to the conversation, is as
follows :—“ Sullivan, as is his custom after the show, was
taking in the sights about town with a party of friends
last night and visited, among others, Peter Jackson’s
saloon. Mr. Hess, Peter’s partner, was behind the bar,
and, recognising Sullivan, the conversation naturally
turned on the recent Corbett-Jaokson light. Sullivan
began by saying with Corbett should have whipped Peter
in shorter order, and frequently referred to Peter hi
very abusive terms. ‘ Corbett ought to have licked him
sooner, and I wish he had, for I don’t like the coon, and I
don’t think he’s a lighter either.’ It appears that other
ears than Hess’s overheard the conversation, and those
ears belonged to Jackson. Jackson had been attending the
Gregaines-Turner contest, and returning to his saloon had
gone into one of the side rooms. He heard Sullivan’s talent
enter and listened to Sullivan’s conversation until he
could stand it no longer. Walking out into the bar-
room Jackson went deliberately up to the big fellow’ pre-
tending not to know him, and, tapping him on the hack,
said; ‘Look here, young man, you don’t know what you’re
talking about. You’re talking through your hat.’
Sullivan glared like a lion, but Jackson didn’t flinch.
Sullivan evidently didn’t know he was talking to Jack-
son and, giving him a contemptuous lookj roared
‘Who are you talking to? I don’t stand that sort of
thing from your sort of people.’ ‘ Well,’said Jackson,
determinedly, ‘ I’m talking to you and I repeat that you
are talking through your hat.’ A fight looked imminent
at this moment, and Sullivan’s friends stepped in between
the two men and tried to separate them. Jackson, how-
ever, would not listen to the peacemakers and repeated
to Sullivan, ‘You are talking through your hat.’
Sullivan at once took off his coat, but Jackson, quick as
u flash, opened his vest and was on the point of pulling
off both coat and vest when friends again crowded round
Sullivan and induced him to leave the saloon, and lie
didn’t leave unwillingly either. Hess cooled Jackson off,
and in five minutes everything w’as going as usual.”

Around De Mestre’s name the turfite wreaths a
host of recollections. Only a few’ years back he had over
100 thoroughbreds in training, or under his care.
Romance exudes from the period when De Mestre, Tait,
Wilson (of St. Albans) and Hurtle Fisher fought it out on
the turf.
# - # *
On the tombstone of Geo. Williams, the N.Z.
lightweight jockey, “knocked out” by liquor, are the
words, supplied in advance by little Williams himself,
“ Too fast to last.”
# * *
Yet another pugilistic farce. There is a high
admission-fee. The men are trained to the hour. Their
muscles stand out like ropes and the pugs seem fit to
fight for hours for a prize that some years back would
have been a little fortune. Yet what do we find? A lot
of sparring, and one goes down to a hit under the jaw,
rolls over and stays there until after the call of time.
Like pony-racing, this business is about played out, apd
so it will prove w’hen the public comes to know that the
side-wagers are in most cases put up by certain members
of certain clubs. Pugs walk about now in broadcloth and
tall hats, put up at the best hotels, and make stacks
of money. When the public come to size them up
properly the halls will be “ to let.”

tremendous impost of lOst. 121bs.

Judging from the accounts by the mail of the
Jackson-Corbett fight, Jackson appears to be out of it as
a champion, having apparently lost the rather small
amount of “devil” he formerly possessed in his hitting
power, to back up his wonderful boxing ability. It is
pretty evident that he fought for a draw.
tt » »
Slavin has now reached the top rung of the
slugging ladder, and, to our way of thinking, no Yankee
or "Englishman who is not tired of life will try to shift
him. That good-tempered, quiet lamb, Patrick Francis
Card. Slavin, will find things hang a bit, unless another
steam battering-ram can be prevailed upon to cross the
stream and explain that there is no room for two of their
class on this earth. It would indeed be sport for the
gods. We would back the house against any two of the
Divine Sara’s or a dozen of Varley’s. Let the posters be
worded:—“Slavin v. Goddard. One to be killed or no
fight. Admission, £lO 10s. For gentlemen only.”
# # *
A certain Melbourne man has given away more
prizes for slugging exhibitions than all the other sports
together in Australia, and in return he asks no more
than that he may be allowed to stand referee. Truly
some rich men are queer cranks.

Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimpeded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.
In selling this Pamphlet we are obliged to re-
require a “Pledge of Honor” from those purchas-
ing it, not to show or reveal its contents outside
of their own families. This is for our own protec-
tion, and as the only means by which our just
rights in the discovery can be maintained.
Doctors, of course, who purchase are not debarred
from using the treatment in their own practice,
providing they do not show the pamphlet or do not
reveal the rationale of the treatment.

DR. R. E. STEVENS’ INDORSEMENT.
Dr. Stevens, of Syracuse, N.Y., was the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment —
more than 20 years ago. In writing to a friend,
about a year ago, he gives the following :
***** “Nearly twenty years ago in this city Dr. A.
Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed history of a
hygienic treatment he had discovered and adopted some
twenty years' before, and which he had practised con-
tinuously upon himself up to that time. The history
included an account of the failure of the health, the
reasonings and conclusions which led him to adopt the
treatment, his rapid restoration to health, &c. From the
time of his first mention of it to me I have been entirely
satisfied that it saved him from a lingering consumption
and death ; and that in the years following, as I have evi-
dence to satisfy me, its continued practice has so affected
the nutrient and eliminating functions as to give to his
physical structure a greater degree of solidity, or, in
other words, a higher specific gravity than that possessed
by any other man living. I learn that he has persistently
continued the treatment to the present time—forty years
in all—and that his present health and vigorous condition
may rightfully be compared to the ‘bloom of youth,’
though he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in my prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due to him for
whatever scientific advances he may be able to unfold in
the line of assisting nature to ward off disease, and in the
restoration of health.— R. F. Stevens, M.D.”

Labour Disputes and the Way Out.
The worker in glutted centres of population looks
longingly to the sheepwalk that has so many
potentialities for him if it were only his. Bat
unfortunately private property in land compels
the pinched city operative to tread in the old
groove for his crust and to die in the h im jsj that
social conditions have fitted on him. The dis-
content of that thwarted worker has found ex-
pression in many forms, and now a workman—a
shearer and miner and withal a scholar and a
business genius, one Arthur Tremayne—has. after
12 months’hard work, succeeded in giving practical
form to a radical remedy for the distresses of the
city-bound wage-earner— and thisremedy is nothing
more nor less than a Co-operativelrrigation Colony.
The institution is now on a thoroughly sound
basis and has been registered un.der the Provident
Societies Act as the Co-operative Irrigation and
Mercantile Society of Australia. The directorate
consists of 18 members made up of six of each of
the representatives of Co-operative Capitalism,
Trades and Labour Unions, and Government
officials and Friendly Societies, and includes Pro ■
fessor Kernot, of Melbourne University ;W. G.
Spence, of the Miners’ and Shearers’ Unions ; and
Alex. Sutherland, M.A., Carlton College. Mr.
Cowdery, Vice-President of the Melbourne Cham-
ber of Commerce, and the Hon. Simon Fraser are
also prospective directors. The credit of formu-
lating and bringing into active operation the
scheme is entirely due to Mr. Tremayne, man iger
of the Society, and after him to the representa-
tives of capital, who have by their present action
evidenced a desire to adopt the profit-sharing
system. The first issue of the Society’s shares is
£IOO,OOO of £1 each, most of which have been
allotted. The objects of the Society are to
settle the people oh irrigated land, to compass
a more equitable distribution of profits between
the capitalist and the labourer, and to reduce to
actual cost the prices of necessaries to members
and settlers. The society is endeavouring to
secure a tract of 250,000 acres of magnificent soil
on the river Murray, a stream emptying seven
billions of gallons daily in the rainy season into
Lake Alexandrina. Other colonies will be later
established in other portions of Australia, and
we may yet see as a result of this truly national
undertaking the deceptive Darling locked for all
its length, with its waters backed up in
irrigating channels and the once-baked soil of its
banks and flats blooming with flowers, and run-
ning wine and oil (olive, by the way). Already
three colonies are in working order and partly
settled, viz., Riverside (Vic.), 26 families (12 non-
resident), and planted with 23,800 raisin-grape
vines, 20,000 cuttings same, 4000 Zante currants,
912 apricots, (550 figs, 528 peaches, 565
prunes, 450 pears, 360 apples, 100 quince,
70 cherries, 100 oranges and lemons, and 350
almonds. The other colonies, Torrumbarry
(Vic.) and Mount Arapiles (Vic.) are also par-
tially settled and planted. The existing colonies
and the larger ones to be started are and will be
worked on the same basis as those at Mildura and
Renmark, with this difference —that while a
definite interest of 10 per cent, will be paid to
capital, the nett profits will be divided amongst
the workers. The system actually means the
federation of labour and capital secured for the
first time in Australia. The business of the
Society is being developed in a most thorough
manner. Two representatives have been sent to
England, one being stationed in the north, the
centre of the Co-operative people, and the other
in London. There are over 20 branch offices in
Australian provincial towns, and these will have
the charge of an enormous staff appointed to
canvas 250,000 of the Australian workers.
The management informs ns that snb-agents
are wanted in every town where not now
represented. The rules of the Society are
simple revelations —part of all that goes from
it comes back to it. Wholesale co-operative
stores are to be opened on the vaii jus settlements,
so that the settler shall pay only actual cost for his
necessaries, and then participate in the profits on
goods sold to the misguided man who is not a
member. Ten per cent, of every shilling paid by
the Society in wages must be invested in the
Society or its land, and the Workers’ Bank is
ready to received on deposit all that can be spared
from the remainder. No person can hold more
than 200 shares, nor transfer any of them—if a
member desires to withdraw, the Society calls in
the shares and pays him the value thereof to
date. Every shilling arising from the sale of
township lots will be utilised for development
purposes, or for improving the township itself.
The irrigated lands can be purchased outright,
or by instalments extending over a term of
years—the purchaser can work his purchase per-
sonally, or have it done for him by the Society’s
labourers, so that he need not visit his property
until it has become a productive one. And if a
man has no money, but only an ability and will-
ingness to work, the Society says it can afford to
take him in hand all the same, and requests him
to write. The uses and prospective products of
this irrigated land are legion. Oranges, for
instance—6o trees go to the acre, £lO to £l4 per
tree per season is a fair average in America, and a
tree will bear for two centuries. Olives have
produced at San Diego (Cal.) 250 gallons of oil to
the acre, and oil is worth £1 per gallon, and is
becoming more valuable as Australians decrease
indulgence in that irrational and English habit of
inordinate meat-_eating. The olive is the great
substitute for animal food in Southern Europe—
Franco produces £20,000,000 worth of oil a year.
The Winegrape—the largest vineyard in France—
is 54 acres in area, which produces £20,000 a year
in wine. Australia imports £IOO,OOO Worth of
raisin-grapes annually, and that subject to an
import tax of twopence per lb.; and the annual
value of imported canned and dried fruits is half
a million of money. These figures can’t lie. We
have no fruit or vegetable canning or dried-fruit
factories in Australia to speak of, no more than
we have a rational market-garden not tilled
by Chinamen or a chicken-raising farm worked on
sensible lines. There is an irrigated garden in
Adelaide that could give the produce of the
Eastern Provinces points and win easily. Besides
all the well-known fruits, great silk-skinned
apricots, hazel, filbert, and Barcelona nuts, limes,
walnuts, almonds, shaddocks, cumquats, and
guavas grow in profusion, and pumpkins 1501bs. in
weight have been frequently turned out. Mr. W.
B. Chaff ey said four years back : “I expect to see
a million and a-half acres of land tilled on the
Murray before long, and if the supply is assured,
I have no doubt as to the call of the market.”
The man with a 10-acre lot of irrigated country
under cultivation with a matchless climate such
as the Murray provides, is a more prosperous and
independent man than the ordinary city shop-
keeper, and can attain a condition of independence
that is, in this age of competition, ideal. #

PRICE-ONE SHILLING.
The Remittance Man's Alimentary
Annuity.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There is a halo of glory around the man who
gets money from home.” There are many such
men who are always getting (present participle)
mo , n .ey which never arrives. One such impostor is
a hideous German, who has come into several
fortunes. He did not receive the last one (or any
other) because the Council had told him he would
have to go home to get it, and, being a deserter,
• e J?' rec * Spandau or other military prisons,
ihis Deutscher is worth describing, although he
has nothing to do with our poor dead Remittance
Man. German Bill is six feet in height and his
colour is that of a patient who has recovered from
yellow-fever under a fearful salivation by mercury,
ills nose is straight down from his forehead until
within half an inch of the end and then it shoots
out at right angles. He is never tidy, never clean,
always ugly and, of course par 'consequence, a
cook. But he has redeeming points: he can
bring out a low note from the depths of the
pylorus and frighten children into fits ; and he has
IV.To, Fntz,’ that smokes a pipe and waltzes to
the BlaueDanube.” To Capricorn with German
Bill, let us tackle oub man. I give you the
etory as he told it to me a few days
a ?°> °V; V l6 of May. Being an admirer
ot truth, but at the same time having a holy
horror of profanity, I do not know how to give
his exact words without doing injury to my feel-
ings and perhaps shocking the ears of my more
sensitive readers. However, imagine me in a
traveller s hut at Rumgong, and making tea and
damper for a poor fellow sick unto death. This is
how he went on :
“ A shame a disgrace. To think that the sneak-
ing, prayerful, hypocritical Scotch lawyers and
elders or deacons or, blast them, whatever they
are should have persuaded my old uncle, the
Bishop, to leave such an iniquitous will. Bishop !
no, he wasn’t a bishop, you idiot. We’ve no
bishops in the Presbyterian Church. But my
sister Molly, Mary Dempster, gave him that nick-
name, and I, Andrew Dempster, used to address
**' ,n Rs My Lord, or sometimes, James Crossford.
1 hat s where he lived and died. IPs wife, my
aunt, left me a few hundreds. The Dari in”'
whalers know how I spent it; and soon the news
went home, and the outcome was that instead of
getting a hundred or two, with which I could
have even gone home and perhaps had a shake of
the elbow at Monte Carlo, he’s left me the sura of
seven hundred to purchase an annuity. Rest
ms soul in peace! He knew not what he
did, and I forgive him, yea, and until seventy
times seven. I think I’m off my dot. Isn’t it
a sign of approaching dissolution when you begin
thinking of boyhcd ? Ju t now I saw Geordy
Drysdale and Tom Hodge and Annie
MacLellan and all the lassies listening to the
Bishop’s open-air preaching, just as distinctly as
i see you. L>ut I must tell you before Igo to
S e t e^7 1 0,0 * eel s^epy— about this annuity.
‘Oh! the wording of that will and the
horrible, pawky prolixity of the lawyer's letter.
Ihe trustees—d n them !—are my own first
cousins. If they had been insuring my life they’d
haveliad me examined, and I don’t believe any
office would take it. But they first handed those
seven solid centuries to an insurance-office, who
coolly tell me I’m to live twenty years. Shall I
see twenty months? or weeks? or days? oi
hours? And, listen, the annuity is to be strictly
alimentary. For alimentation they send me a
paper to fill up and sign before a banker. I’ve
done so. Ihe whisky and exes, waiting round
that bank cost me one half-year’s payment. And
I can’t forestall on mortgage that miserable divi-
dend. There’s one clause in the will that’s a bit
of balm to my wounded soul. It’s not attachable by
my creditors ! I hat’s the old boy’s doing. He
know me. So now I have to go whaling till that
paper goes to Crossford, and thence to London
and, curse them, it’s got to be uplifted in Edin-
burgh. What they’ll do then I don’t know.
Probably send it out here, addressed to Rumgong
Sydney, Melbourne, South Australia. They
don’t know antipodean geography in Lanarkshire.
And I’ve got to show myself in Narandera twice
a year. 1 hat’s two drunks. Three or four ought
to kill mo : in fact, have killed me. You’ve read
“ Warren’s Miscellanies, ’’ain’tit, or is itthe ‘‘Diary
of a Late Physician ” ? Well, I’ve got the aura.
When I close my eyes, I’m steepleohasing on
Kilhecrankie, the roan pony, that Colonel, beg
pardon, General Probyn gave me. When he falls,
I end my race. The combat will then be o’er.
Here, lam at the hymns. Last night I repeated
all the psalms I was so often whopped for. Katy
Dunn and .lessie Macfarlane were hearing me
and Peggy Mitchell was knitting in the corner!
She had a bunch of feathers in her apron-strings,
and stuck the spare needles in them. I like
that psalm about waiting more than they that
wait the morning light to see. It puts me
in mind of waiting for the pubs, to open. David
was a boozer. I think I’ll go to Scotland
or India. Good night. A ten-pound saddle just
makes the weight. Let go his head. Throw the
whip at me, 111 catch it. Now, then, Killiecrankie,
I’ll change your name to the Alimentary Annuity!
We’re off.” And with that rare smile on his
placid face, that first showed his white teeth and
caused a slight twitching of the edges of his lips
my old chum Andrew Dempster, of Easter and
Wester Haughs, fell asleep. Time, 1 p.m. At
five he had some tea, no bread or meat, and said
it was.a fast-day because it preceded a feast; the
payments being due on the loth of May and the
11th November. Then he said : “Bust mo if I
know what makes me so sleepy. I think I’ll sleep
my brains away. In fact, I’m cronk and crank. ’,
So he laid his head on his arms and was still until
late in the night. I had gone to bed, but had a
slush-lamp burning. At exactly midnight Andy
flopped down on his bunk and began—“ Amen,
amen, dico vohis: qui in ostia per }Mi'tas non
ingrcditur sed ascendit aliunde hallo, you here
again ? Safe over that one, next is a double.
Alimentary Annuity, late Killiecrankie. Now
the water. Looks broad, deep and shining
Believe it’s the Jordan, or Lethe. It’s the Shining
River. Over ! safe ! ” and Andrew Dempster, late
of the Easter and Wester Haughs, required no
more legacies, Alimentary or other.

FEDERATION OR NO FEDERATION.
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pemell’s Meal.

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
for all scouring and house-cleaning.

Sunday Afternoon in
Melbourne.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Compared with Sydney, Melbourne is
poorly provided with Sunday afternoon resorts;
apart from the popular and übiquitous public-
house, the Sunday-school, and the malodorous but
instructive Zoo., where a lot of discouraged-look-
ing animals and a great variety of dejected birds
in various stages of dyspepsia and decay are open
to inspection from noon till sundown, there isnot,
at this season of the year, a public place within or
without the “ Marvellous ” where the ungodly can
disport with any physical comfort or mental
profit, and, as the Zoo. is apt to pall after a dozen
visits and fifty ineffectual attempts to awake the
crocodile from his oppressive lethargy, and many
fruitless endeavours to make the lion get up and
roar and the tiger dispel his ennui , by poking
his leading features up with a stick, the
thoughtful, middle-aged man, seeking innocent
recreation, falls back in time upon the grog-shop
or the Sunday-school. And again, as all men
cannot take upon themselves to point out to a
class of obtuse and weary small boys wherein they
should and should not emulate the prophets, nor
appreciate the ecstacy of ocular and oscular flirta-
tion with the sweet young thing in the fashionable
hat and gold bangles who teaches moral precepts
to the envious little girls, we find a large and in-
creasing pressure of popularity on the back-
entrance of the pub ; but here again the big beer
and the “dim religious light” fail to fascinate
numerous exacting minds, and we have a great
residue of bored citizens left, who, for the lack of
other attractions, resort to bed on the Sabbath
afternoon, in deep disgust, and contract indiges-
tion and liver-complaint by sleeping directly after
meals.
Very few young men go to Sunday-school in
search of relaxation, and those who do are tall,
pale youths who take to long, black coats and
belltoppcrs very early in life, and eventually be-
come either undertakers, parsons, or lay co-respon-
dents in divorce. Most of the young fellows
lean up against corners, and smoke and hang out
till Monday; but some frequent the Sandridge
railway pier.
The fact that so ghastly a place as the Sand-
ridge Pier is a popular Sunday afternoon resort
should be sufficient evidence of the necessity
of something being done to mitigate -the solemn
stupidity of the Melbourne Sabbath and make the
tax-payer less earnestly wish himself dead on the
day of rest. Sandridge Pier is a barren pile of
planks jutting out into a usually mud-coloured
sea; it has some ships tethered against it, and
commands a view of another pier to the left, a
spread of tainted bay in front, and a great waste
place to the right. Saving when a misguided
shark gets tangled up amongst the piles and
engenders a fleeting and fictitious excitement by
nearly rooting up the whole structure, this resort
is a dull and dreary repetition of last Sunday
afternoon, and has been so for very many weary
years. Yet thither flock the young people on the
seventh day, and endeavour to beguile the lagging
hours by walking solemnly up one side and
strolling soberly down the other— a performance
varied occasionally in the case of young men who
perch along the fence, and seek release from the
prevailing depression by passing painful criti-
cisms on the dress and general deportment of the
mildest and most submissive-looking passers-by.
It is foolish to take to heart any objections a con-
gregation of disinterested young men may express
regarding your boots or the hang of your gar-
ments when you are out for the day. Such objec-
tions really do not moan much: the young men
being bored, and hungry for mental relaxation,
are apt to carp at trifles, but some people feel
those things keenly, and, after being requested to
“ hitch up their pants” or “ let down their socks,”
lose interest in their surroundings and ache to be
home and alone. A young friend of mine went
out in a new belltopper last Sunday afternoon.
It was a beautiful hat, and had cost the young
banker a lot of wages, and he was very proud of
it, indeed. He returned home about an hour
later, looking blighted, and there took his new hat
off and put it on the fire. There was no more
pleasure for him in that hat—he’d been to Sand-
ridge Pier.
» * #
A noticeable feature of the afternoon’s
entertainment on the pier is the fact that the
sexes keep themselves severely apart. In a few
isolated instances a young gallant may be seen
accompanying a chosen fair in her peregrinations
up and down, but he is ignorant of the manners
and customs of the place, and so palpably a
stranger and a “mug,” that he comes in for more
than ordinary attention and playful .comment.
The regular attendants do not run in couples; the
young men and the maidens seems to know each
other, and exchange facetious allusions in passing,
such as—“ How do, Poll ? Where did yer spring
the beaver?” or, “Strike me fat, Kitty, you are
a bit of stuff!” to which pleasantries the ladies
make chaste and appropriate response, but the
gentlemen in their nonchalance and lassitude ex-
tend their courtesies no further, being evidently too
confident of the strength of their positions in the
estimation of the other sex to condescend to
trifling conventionalities.
For years the pier has been regarded as a public
rostrum, to which frowsy individuals of peculiar
grammar and every shade and grade of opinion
may repair to air their sentiments and come down
in all the fury of wild rhetoric and mixed metaphor
on things celestial and terrestrial. There the burn-
ing question of Jonah and the whale has been
settled in accordance with all phases of belief and
unbelief, existing governments have been flaunted
—stripped of all subterfuge—in their ghastly
nakedness before the eyes and minds of the pro-
letariat, whilst most people drifted past and never
seemed to hear the various tidings of great joy or
heed the zealous declaimers.
One Sunday, when I was younger, I visited the
pier and heard a large dark man with the configu-
ration of a melo-dramatic outlaw, roaring from a
pile in hysterical fury, and I asked where were
the police—l wanted to give the man in charge
for being illegally at large in the colony and a
menace to life and limb. When the police came
they told me he was an itinerant evangelist preach-
ing the gospel of peace, and quite harmless accord-
ing to the highest authorities. I was surprised.
£
Of late, however, the pier has been robbed o 1
the little diversity which such interesting propa-
gandists afforded it, and now only a temperance
fanatic of wonderful staying powers and an aged
and uninteresting lady who has started out on a
Social Purity basis, bearing a snow-white banner
with an auger rampant, and who occasionally
collects herself on an eminence to discourse on
delicate questions, contend to break the placid
flow of people wandering aimlessly up one side and
drifting wearily down the other.
The Social Purity enthusiast is a spare, iron-
grey lady with a fixed eye that glares straight
ahead like a policeman’s lantern in a pantry, and
the grounds of her complaint are the suppositions
that Melbourne is a sink of iniquity, and that
there is a lot of disguised infamy going on in high
places. She proposes wiping the town clean,
abolishing all the bold, bad men, and starting a-
fresh.
The man who arises with unswerving regularity
to pass a few remarks about the curse of drink is
a familiar feature. He is the same old bore who
has been rising to remark since the time of
Moses, and he has already succeeded in con-
vincing several people that the curse of drink
—the dreadful outcome of the pernicious
habit of drinking alcoholic stimulants, so pre-
valent in modern society, is the temperance
orator. lam one of Lis converts. Nobody stops
to listen to this crank now, except three or four
dazed individuals who come with him and seem
to be fascinated by the power of his awful jaw;
but he does not care particularly about an au-
dience —he only wants to get up there and talk ; it
is necessary for his complaint that he should do
so, and if he can run on for an hour or two unin-
terruptedly he is quite content. He takes his
stand at the head of the pier, closes his eyes to
external circumstances, and lets his feelings loose;
and he leaks ruinous, heart-breaking statistics,
and ghastly examples, and a great, oppressive
incubus of figures as long as his information lasts,
and his several admirers stand round, under the
influence, with their mouths wide open to grasp
the situation, and look like an asylum out for an
airing. Meanwhile the people come and go un-
awed by the gruesome prognostications of the
prophet of slops. Up and back! Up and
back!! Let us join in the “demnition grind,”
and enjoy the full advantages of this popular
resort —one of the few that puritanical Melbourne
KXBJ

Thank heaven for a little diversion a yo g
woman has jumped into the bay.
The crowd at once sets to work to naui
would-be suicide out again. A crowd is always
meddlesome and enterprising.
The girl was fished out, and had to appear
before the Court next morning. She said that her
lover had deserted her, and she was in despair.
But that was all nonsense—she merely wished to
escape the awful depression of a Melbourne Sun-
day.
